Mularz\nPropulsion Directorate\nU.S. Army Aviation Research and Technology Actlvlty-AVSCOM\nNASA Lewis Research Center\nCleveland, Ohio\n\nAt the NASA Lewis Research Center, combustion research is being conducted\nin the Internal Fluid Mechanics Division.\nThe research is organized into three\nmain functions focusing on the fluid dynamics related to aeropropulslon\nsystems\n(fig. l).\nThe first function, computational methods, looks at improved algorithms and new computational fluid dynamic techniques to solve Internal flow\nproblems, including heat transfer and chemical reactions.\nThls area also looks\nat using expert systems and parallel processing as they might be applied to\nsolving internal flow problems.\nThe second function is fundamental experiments.\nThese experiments can\ngenerate benchmark data in support of computational models and numerical codes,\nor they can focus on the physical phenomena of interest to obtain a better\nunderstanding\nof the physics or chemistry involved as a preamble to models and\ncomputer codes.\nComputational\napplications is the third function in the Internal Fluid\nMechanics Division.\nNew flow codes are validated against available experimental data, and they are used as a tool to investigate performance of real\nengine hardware.\nSince the geometry may be quite complex for the system being\nanalyzed, large grids and computer storage may be required.\nThe hardware of\ninterest includes combustion chambers, high-speed inlets, and turbomachlnery\ncomponents (e.g., a centrifugal compressor).\nThe goal of this research is to bring computational\nfluid dynamics to a\nstate of practical application for the aircraft engine industry.\nAs shown in\nfigure 2, the approach is to have a strongly integrated computational\nand\nexperimental program for all the disciplines associated with the gas turbine\nand other aeropropulslon\nsystems by advancing the understanding\nof flow\nphysics, heat transfer, and combustion processes.\nThe computational\nand\nexperimental\nresearch is integrated In the following way:\nthe experiments that\nare performed provide an emplrlcal data set so that physical models can be\nformulated to describe the processes that are occurring - for example, turbulence or chemical reaction.\nThese experiments also form a data base for those\nwho are doing code development by providing experimental data against which\nthe codes can be verified and assessed.\nModels are generated as closure to\nsome of the numerical codes, and they also provide physical insight for experiments.\nAt the same time, codes which solve the complete Navler-Stokes\nequations can be used as a kind of numerical experiment from which far more\nextensive data can be obtained than ever could be obtained experimentally.\nThis could provide physical insight into the complex processes that are taking\nplace.\nThese codes are also exercised against experimental data to assess the\naccuracy and applicability\nof models (e.g., the turbulence model).\nWe feel\nthat a fully integrated computatlonal-experlmental\nresearch program Is more\nproductive than other approaches and that It is the most desirable way of\npursuing our goal.\n\nFigure 3 is a cutaway view of a hypothetical combustor which illustrates\nthe typical complex fluid mechanics and combustion features.\nThe flows are\nhighly three-dlmenslonal with turbulence levels, in many cases, comparable in\nmagnitude to the bulk velocity.\nLiquid fuels are injected as a spray which\nthen undergoes vaporization and mixing. The chemical reaction which occurs\ncauses changes in density and fluid mechanics properties and can cause the\nformation of a solid phase (soot) with its attendant hlgh-radlatlon heat\ntransfer.\nAn understanding of these physical processes is needed before\naccurate numerical codes can be built and used as a predictive tool in the\ndesign process. In addition, the numerical methods for three-dlmenslonal\nflows need improvements in accuracy and efficiency in order to properly\nsimulate the features of these flows.\nThis then is the framework of the combustion research program. As shown\nin figure 4, the program is divided into four elements:\nadvanced numerics,\nfuel sprays, fluid mixing, and radiation chemistry.\nThe research in each of\nthese elements is focused on the long-range objective of developing numerical\ncodes that can be used as a predictive tool to describe both two- and threedimensional flows.\nOnce the codes can be used with confidence,\nindustry will\nbe able to integrate them into their combustor design system.\nBefore discussing the specific research currently taking place, I would\nlike to describe the major computing hardware now being used at NASA Lewis to\ncompute reacting internal flows. Lewis currently has a CRAY 1-S computer which\nis tied to an IBM 370 system for Input/output.\nIt has a relatively small\nstorage capacity, but a very hlgh calculation speed.\nIn the near future a\nCRAY X-MP will be installed (fig. 5).\nIt will replace the CRAY l-S and will\nprovide a modest increase in computer speed.\nCore storage, however, will\nincrease dramatically from 2 million words to 36 million words, and a highspeed solld-state device, which is practically equivalent to core memory, will\nalso be included.\nIn addition, the new equipment will have two processors\nwhich will give Lewis some ability to start using algorithms that can take\nadvantage of simultaneous processing and thus increase the computation speed\neven further.\nIn addition to the computers located at Lewis, NASA is building a "super\ncomputer" at its Ames Research Center called the Numerical Aerodynamic\nSimulator (NAS) (fig. 6).\nThe NAS computer will be accessed at both NASA\nLangley and NASA Lewis through satellite linkage with a UNIX operating system.\nIn its current configuration, the NAS computer is a CRAY 2 with 256 megawords\nof memory, which will enable it to make very large calculations of chemically\nreacting internal fluid flows.\nIt will have four processors.\nIn addition, as\nthe state-of-the-art\nof computers advances changes will be made in the NAS\ncomputer to reflect these advances.\nThe current research in combustion can be summarized in six major activities.\nThe first actlvlty is called improved numerical methods for complex\nI\n\nIUW_.\n\nIIUIII_I\n\nIbal\n\n_l\n\nI Ul\n\nI_\n\na\n\nUI_\n\n_1UUI_III\n\nWILII\n\nLII_\n\nbU!\n\nI_IIL\n\nIIUIII_I\n\nI_OI\n\n_UU_3.\n\nThis numerical\nerror must be reduced before\nthe accuracy\nof physical\nmodels can\nbe assessed.\nThe grids\nused in calculations\nthat\nare performed\nwith\nstate-ofthe-art\ncodes are very sensitive\nto the flow\ndirection\nrelative\nto that\ngrid,\nand at high angles\nthis\nsensitivity\nleads to serious\nnumerical\nerrors.\nTechniques to reduce numerical\ndiffusion,\nor error,\nand to virtually\neliminate\nit\nfrom calculations\nare being pursued.\nWork is also underway to establish\nsecond-order\naccurate\nclosure\nmodels\nfor turbulent\nreacting\nflow.\n\nThe second activity\nInvolves\nthe development\nof techniques\nfor making\npredictive\ncalculations\nof chemically\nreacting\nflow.\nOne of the more promising\ntechniques,\nwhich\nis really\nIn tts\ninfancy,\nis called\ndirect\nnumerical\nslmulatlons,\nor DNS.\nIn this\ntechnique,\nthe Navler-Stokes\nequations\nare solved\ndlrectly\nwithout\nany modeling\nof the turbulence.\nThis technique\nis currently\nbeing applied\nto reacting\nshear layers.\nAlthough\nthis\nIs a relatively\nsimple\nflow,\nthere\nis nevertheless\na lo\xc2\xb1 of complexity\nassociated\nwith\nit.\nMuch work\nremains to be done with\nthis\ntechnique,\nbut the results\nto date have been very\npromising.\nAnother technique that has been used In several kinds of flows Is called\nthe random vortex method.\nThis technique accounts for the vortlclty generated\nat the wall of the confined flow and solves the vortlclty equation without any\nturbulence closure modeling.\nCalculated results of flow over a rearward\nfacing step show many of the characteristics\nseen In hlgh-speed movies of\nturbulent reacting flow experiments.\nThe third activity involves benchmark experiments for code development\nand\nverification.\nTwo-phase flow research Is currently underway.\nDetailed data\nare required for code assessment, and instruments which Lewis has helped to\ndevelop now show much promise of being able to make the appropriate measurements.\n(Those instruments are also being used to support icing research.)\nResearch is also being conducted on numerical calculation of two-phase flow.\nWlth the data from the experiments, an assessment of the current code capability wlll be made to guide future code development efforts.\nIn addition, an\nextensive set of experiments is being conducted to look at the mixing of\ndilution Jets into a cross stream in a channel.\nA substantial range of parametric variables has been studied, and a Very complete set of data has been\nestablished.\nThe next activity Is computer code applications.\nCurrently available\ncodes are applied to real systems.\nThe study of flow In the transition section of a reverse flow combustor is an example of such work.\nHere a flow In\nwhich fuel has already been burned has to undergo a 180 \xc2\xb0 annular turn.\nVery\nstrong secondary flows arise, and the analysis Is very complex.\nAnother\nexample Is the application of the Lewls-developed general chemical kinetics\nprograms and chemical equilibrium codes to problems of practical interest.\nThese codes are used throughout the world.\nThe next activity Is code development for thermochemlcal\nproperties and\nklnetlc rates.\nThermodynamic\ncalculations for real gases are being performed\nIn support of future combustion models.\nThe modeling of chemical kinetic computations for complex reactions Is also being actively developed In support of\nfuture computer codes.\nThe final activity In the current combustion research program Is to\nadvance the understanding\nof chemical mechanisms In reacting flow.\nChemical\nkinetic rates are measured using a shock tube facility.\nThe data from thls\nexperiment are used to assess the kinetic models for various fuels of interest\nIn aeropropulslon\nsystems.\nDetailed characteristics\nof one- and two-dlmensional controlled flames are also being established.\nWe are interested in\nstudying the behavior of some of the minor species of flames and In looking at\nthe soot nucleation growth and eventual soot consumption\nin flames.\nRadiation\nheat transfer Is dominant In combustors and other aeropropulslon\nsystems.\nThe\ncontrol of soot nucleation and growth is essential if radiation heat transfer\n3\n\nis to be reduced. Also, some of the minor intermediates o f combustion are\nbeing measured in an attempt to understand the detailed physics involved in\nflame fronts.\n\nBENCHMARK EXPERl MENTS\n\nCOMPUTATIONAL METHODS\n\nA PPL ICAT I ONS\nFigure 1 - Fundamental aeropropulsion computational and experimental research in\n.\nthe internal fluid mechanics division.\n\nNUMERICAL\n\nEXPERIMENTS\n\nINSIGHT\nMODELING\n\nDEVELOPMENT\n\nNUMERICAL\nCLOSURE\n\n-\n\nCD-5-IMI\n\nFigure 2 Integrated computational-experimental methodology for propulsion\n.\naerothermodynamics research. COMBUSTION\n\nPROCESSES\n\nIN AFTERBURNERS\n\nZhou Xlaoqlng\nChinese Aeronautical Establishment\nBelJlng, People\'s Republic of China\nand\nZhang Xlaochun\nShenyang Aeroenglne Research\nShenyang, People\'s Republic\n\nInstitute\nof China\n\nMathematical models and numerical methods are presented for computer\nmodeling of aeroenglne afterburners.\nA computer code GEMCHIP is described\nbriefly.\nThe algorithms SIMPLER, for gas flow predictions, and DROPLET, for\ndroplet flow calculations, are incorporated in this code.\nThe block correction\ntechnique is adopted to facilitate convergence.\nThe method of handling\nirregular shapes of combustors and flameholders is described.\nThe predicted\nresults for a Iow-bypass-ratlo\nturbofan afterburner in the cases of gaseous\ncombustion and multlphase spray combustion are provided and analyzed, and\nengineering guides for afterburner optimization are presented.\n\nINTRODUCTION\nThus far the design and development of alr-breathlng\nengine combustors\nhave been based mainly on ad hoc tests.\nBut the past lO years have seen a\nboom of numerical fluid mechanics and combustion.\nThe computer modeling technique is beginning to establish itself in territory which has been dominated\nexclusively\nby the empirical technique.\nIt is believed that the combination\nof numerical modeling with the seml-emplrlcal method and the modern diagnostic\ntechnology will greatly promote combustion study and combustor design and\ndevelopment.\nThe objective of this paper (which is part of the authors\' continuing\neffort to make computer models of multlphase turbulent combustion processes in\ncombustors and furnaces (refs. 1 and 2 and private communication with X. Zhang\nand H.H. Chlu, University of Illinois at Chlcago, 1984)) is to present mathematical models and numerical methods for combustor flows and to apply these\nmethods to the computer prediction of aeroenglne afterburner characteristics.\nThe long-ignored droplet-turbulent-dlffuslon\nmodel plays an important role\nin droplet dispersion and species distribution.\nIt has now been incorporated\nin this modeling.\nThe K-c turbulence model is modified and extended to account\nfor multlphase turbulence effects.\nA hybrid, finlte-rate\nchemlcal-reaction\nmodel based on a global Arrhenlus law and a turbulent mlxlng-rate model is used\nJudiciously to predict the combustion rate of multlphase premlxed turbulent\nflames.\nA system of conservation equations of the Eulerlan type is used for\nboth gaseous flow and multlslzed droplet flow predictions.\nTo solve these\nequations, the authors developed a computer code called GEMCHIP (general,\nelllptlc-type,\nmultlphase, combustion-heat-transfer,\nand Interdlffuslon\nprogram), in which the SIMPLER algorithm (ref. 3) for gaseous flow prediction\n\nand the DROPLET\n(private communication with X. Zhou and H.H. Chlu, University\nof Illinois\nat Chicago, IgB3) procedure for droplet flow calculation were\nincorporated.\nThe block correction technique and the alternatlng-dlrectlon\nreclprocatlng-sweep llne-by-llne\nTDMA\n(trl-dlagonal\nmatrix algorithm) method\nwas adopted to facilitate\nconvergence of the iteration processes.\nThe afterburner under study is a Iow-bypass-ratlo turbofan engine augmentor of the axlsymmetrlc type with a bluff body flameholder.\nThe method of\nsimulating the irregularly shaped flow domain and the flameholder configuration\nis described briefly and the inlet and boundary conditions are presented.\nThe\nusual wall functions (ref. 4) are used to bridge the near wall regions where\nlocal Reynolds numbers are very low.\nThe numerical results of gas flow, droplet flow fields, and the spray\nflame structures are presented.\nA study of the parametric sensitivity of the\ncombustion efficiency is made for both gas combustion and multlphase combustion.\nEngineering guides are given for afterburner design optimization.\n\nSYMBOLS\nCp\n\nspecific\n\nheat of gas at constant\n\nE\n\nactivation\n\nh\n\ntotal construct elllptlc-type\nconservation\nequations for both gaseous and droplet phases in two-dlmenslonal,\nmultlphase,\ncombustlng, turbulent flows.\nNondlmenslonallzatlon\nof these equations leads\nto the following two general forms (ref. l and private communication\nwith X.\nZhou and H.H. that the combustion rate\nis controlled by the slower of two competitive rates of successive subprocesses\nthe chemical reaction rate and the turbulent mixing rate.\nThe former rate is\ndetermined by (-E/RuTg)\n\nMETHODS\n\nA 42 by 38 staggered grid system is superimposed on the flow domain.\nThe\npreviously mentioned two general forms of nonlinear partial differential equations are then dlscretlzed into their finite difference counterparts by using\nthe control volume scheme and the primitive variables of pressure and velocity.\nThe power law scheme (ref. 3) is adopted to determine combined diffusion/\nconvection fluxes.\nThe SIMPLER procedure is employed to predict gaseous flow\nfields, it reduces computer time by 30 to 50 percent in comparison with the\npopular SIMPLE procedure.\nThe DROPLET procedure is used for calculating droplet flow fields.\nThe alternatlng-directlon\ndouble-reclprocatlng-sweep\nllneby-line TDMA method is adopted to solve simultaneous algebraic equations Iteratlvely.\nThe block correction technique is used to facilitate convergence.\nThe usual wall function method is employed to bridge the near wall regions\nwhere the laminar viscosity effect is quite strong.\nThis method is very effective in greatly reducing computer time while obtaining rather satisfactory\nresults.\nAll these methods have been assembled into the computer code GEMCHIP.\nThe main flow chart of GEMCHIP is shown in figure I.\nA droplet-free solution is first obtained through SIMPLER, which provides\nboth final results for the single gas-phase flow and the initial guesses of the\ngas flow fields for muitiphase flow.\nThen we enter DROPLET to calculate droplet flow fields and the Interphase exchanges of mass, momentum, and energy.\nThese quantities are substituted as source terms into SIMPLER, and the gas flow\nfields are modified.\nThen we enter DROPLET again.\nThis alternating Iteratlve\nprocess continues until all the gas- and droplet-flow\nequations satisfy the\nconvergence criterion in all grids.\nThe typical iteration number ranges from\n40 to 75 for gas-phase burning problems and from 120 to 150 for multlphase\nburning problems.\nThe corresponding\ncomputer times are about 5 and 30 mln,\nrespectively,\non an IBM 4342 machine.\n12\n\n-\n\nTHE AFTERBURNER\n\nSTUDIED\n\nFigure 2 shows a Iow-bypass-ratlo\nturbofan afterburner In which the computer modeling is made.\nThe afterburner consists of a cone-type dlffuser/mIxer,\na cylindrical combustion section, and a slngle-rlng bluff-body flameholder.\nA\ncylindrical partition board separates the cold fan flow from the hot core flow.\nThe temperature and velocity are 350 K and BO m/sec in the fan flow and 600 to\n800 K and 60 to 200 m/sec In the core flow.\nThe liquid fuel Is evenly In_ected\nin spray form into the core flow, and then the droplets are heated, vaporized,\nmixed, and combusted with oxygen in the airflow.\n\nHandling\n\nof Irregularly\n\nShaped\n\nWall and Flameholder\n\nThe curved afterburner wall and the flameholder are simulated by blocking\noff some of the control volumes of the regular grld system so that the remaining active control volumes form the desired shape.\nThe advantage of thls\nmethod is that a computer code developed for the nominally regular domain can\nbe adapted to flows of arbitrarily shaped configurations\nso that there is no\nneed to generate new programs and the time for developing and testing such\nprograms is saved.\nThe irregular boundary can be represented approximately\nby a series of\nrectangular\nsteps (fig. 3).\nUsually a rather crude representation\nof the\nboundary can produce surprisingly good results.\nThe values of the relevant flow parameters should be given in the blockedoff regions.\nThis is done by using extremely large source terms or by assigning extremely large transport coefficients in these inactive control volumes.\nThese inactive grids also participate in the iteration process.\n\nBoundary\n\nConditions\n\nAppropriate boundary conditions are selected to represent the wall regions\nof the afterburner.\nThe following assumptions can be realistically made with\nregard to this kind of selection:\n(1) No sllp at the wall\n(2) Zero turbulent fluctuation at the wall\n(3) Adiabatic and impermeable wall\nIt is reported that the predicted solution Is very sensitive to the inlet\nconditions.\nFor this reason, the inlet profiles should conform to measured\nvalues whenever available.\nUnfortunately, measurements\nof the profiles of the\ninlet variables, especially the inlet velocity vectors and the inlet turbulence\nvariables\nK and\nc, are seldom carried out in complex geometries.\nIt is,\ntherefore, reasonable to assume the following profiles (ref. 6):\n\nUg = Ugo(1 - F) n\nV\n\n=0\ng\n\n13\n\n1.5\nc = co (K/Ko)\nwhere 0.2 > n > 0 and 2 > m > O. The variables with subscript o denote\nvalues at the centers of cote fTow or fan flow. The nondlmenslonal radius\nT = r/R for the core flow and T = Ir - (R l + Ro)/21/((R1 + Ro)/2) for the\nflow, where\nRo denotes the radius\nthe diffuser\'s inlet radius.\n\nof the partition\n\nboard\n\nand\n\nRl\n\nfan\nrepresents\n\nThe inlet profiles of the droplet number densities, temperatures,\nvelocities vary wlth the fuel nozzle type and spray quality.\n\nand\n\nIn the axis of symmetry the radial gradients\nables are taken to be equal to zero.\n\nvari-\n\nof all droplet-phase\n\nThe exit plane should be located far downstream and outside the reclrculatlon zone so that the local parabolic flow assumption applies and that the\ncalculating domain is isolated from the ambient environment.\nThe only exceptions are the equations for pressure\np and for the pressure correction parameter\np\', since the pressure effect is always two way.\nThe problem is solved\nby calculating the outlet velocity component in the main flow direction.\n\nRESULTS\n\nAND DISCUSSIONS\n\nThe objectives of the numerical study are to analyze the shape and the\nsize of reclrculatlon zones at different working conditions, to examine the\nvaporization process of multlslze fuel sprays, to compare the multlphase flame\nstructures with the gas flame structures, and to study the parametric sensitivity of afterburnlng efficiency.\n\nReclrculation\n\nZones\n\nThe gas velocity vector fields inside the afterburner are shown in\nfigures 4 to 6 for gas flow and multlphase flow.\nThe numerical calculations\nreveal that the reclrculatlon\nzones in the wake of the flameholder are induced\nby negative\n\npressure gradients,\n\nwhich,\n\nin turn, are caused\n\nby gaseous\n\nviscosity.\n\nFigures 4 and 5 are pictures of velocity vectors and X-dlrectlon velocity\ncomponent profiles in a gas phase flow.\nThe length of the reclrculatlon\nzone\nin the cold flow case is about 2.0 to 3.5 times the flameholder width.\nIt Is\nreadily seen from figure 4 and 5 that the wake effect still exists far downstream of the flameholder.\nSome experimental\nreports claim that the reclrculatlon zone wlll uuLu,,_ larger and longer In gas combustlu,, _ases u=_=u_: of gas\nexpansion and the reduction of the absolute value of the negative pressure\nbehind the flameholder.\nBut this trend is not obvious in this calculation.\nResearch work is under way in thls direction.\nFigure 7 shows droplet velocity vectors of two size groups in the multiphase afterburnlng condition.\nA comparison of the multlphase flow with the\ngas flow field (fig. 6) shows that the small droplets are capable of reaching\n14\n\nvelocity\nequilibrium\nmore qutckly\nthan the large droplets.\nThe velocity\nnonequilibrium\nIn the downstream Is somewhat enhanced because the gas is accelerated more rapldly\nthan the droplets.\nThis is especlally\ntrue\nfor the large\ndroplets.\nThe droplet\nreclrculatlon\nzone Is obviously\nsmaller\nthan the gas\nreclrculatton\nzone.\n\nCascading\n\nVaporization\n\nThe preheating and vaporization processes of droplets of different slze\ngroups are illustrated In figure 8. It Is readily seen from the predicted\ncurves that the small droplets (e.g., D1 = 0.15) complete the preheating\nprocess and initiate the vaporization process much earlier than the large\ndroplets (e.g., Dl = 1.60).\nThe phenomenon of successive initiation of the vaporization of droplets\nof different sizes In spray Is designated as cascading vaporization (ref. l)\nsince the initial vaporization\nlines of different slze droplets constitute a\nform of cascade.\n\nGas Flame and Multlphase\n\nFlame\n\nStructures\n\nFigures g(a) and (b) show the temperature field and flame structure of\nthe gas combustion flow.\nThe gas flame structure Is rather simple:\nthe flow\nof the combustible mixture mixes wlth the fan flow of the cold alr, and the\nflame Is stabilized In the reclrculatlon zone close behind the flameholder.\nThere Is a "dark" area, which Is fuel-deflclent,\nIn the wake of the flameholder.\nThls Is where the combustion Is completed.\nThe structure of the multlphase flame Is very complicated.\nCalculation\nreveals that there are three principal combustion modes:\n(1) pre-evaporatlng\nflame, (2) dense spray group flame, and (3) dilute spray group flame.\nThe\ntotal fuel-alr ratio and the spray group combustion number\nGc are the two\nmaln parameters determining combustion mode.\nThe\nGc\nIs actually the ratio\nof the characteristic\nvaporization rate to the convection flow rate and was\nfirst proposed by Prof. H.H. Chlu (ref. 7).\nPre-evaporatlng\nflame. - The pre-evaporatlon\nflame Is shown In figure lO.\nWhen the\nGc number Is very large (fine atomization,\ni.e., the spray consists of an extremely large quantity of small droplets) all the droplets are\nvaporized at a typical afterburner\ninlet temperature\nbefore they get to the\nflame zone.\nThe flame structure Is similar to that of gas phase combustion.\nDense spray qroup flame. - If the Gc\nnumber Is rather large and the\nfuel-air ratio Is also high, dense spray group combustion occurs (fig. ll);\nthls Is characterized\nby the presence of an oxygen-deflclent\nzone, which\ndecreases combustion efficiency.\nBoth types of gas and droplet burning coexist\nin the flame.\nDilute spray qroup flame. - Thls,mode occurs at a small\nGc number, which\nIs characterized\nby poor atomization quality and a low fuel-alr ratio:\nthat\nIs, there Is a small quantity of blg droplets.\nIf the droplet slze Is large\nenough and droplets concentrate near the central llne of the afterburner, the\nv-type flame shown In figure 12 may appear.\nThls type of flame Is unstable.\n15\n\nFigures 13(a) and (b) show, respectively, the axial distributions\nof\nafterburnlng efflclencles in gas-phase and multlphase combustion cases. It is\nseen that the growth of the combustion efficiency for the multlphase flame has\na slower start than that for the gas flame. This difference is caused by the\ndroplet preheating and by vaporization processes. The results also indicate\nthat the smaller the spray group combustion number G the larger the\nc,\ndifference.\nParametric Sensitivity\n\nStudy of Combustion Efficiency\n\nFigure 14 showsthe effect of fuel-alr ratio on gas-phase combustion efficiency at two different pressures (2.0 kg/cm2 and 0.5 kg/cm2). The efflclency curve reaches its peak at an approximately stolchlometrlc fuel-alr\nratio\nin the core flow. At higher pressures, the efficiency peak movestoward richer\nm_xtures and the curve becomesflatter because of the dilution action by the\nfan flow and the improvement of combustion conditions.\nFigure 15 showsthe variation of the multlphase combustion efficiency with\nthe fuel-air ratio at two different spray group combustion numbers. With\nmultlphase combustion there is a combustion efficiency peak at an appropriate\nfuel-alr ratio similar to that with gas-phase combustion. With an increase of\nG number, the efficiency peak movestoward leaner mixtures - which means\nc\nmixtures with a larger number of smaller-slzed droplets will have a higher combustion efficiency at a lower fuel-alr\nratio.\nThe results are also in agreement with the experimental measurements.\nFigures 16(a) and (b) showthe effect of the inlet gas temperature on combustion efflclencles of gas-phase and multlphase flames. The temperature\nincrease enhancesthe heat release rate and speeds up droplet vaporization,\nwhich, in turn, enhancesthe combustion efficlencles\nof both the gas and multiphase flames. But, for obvious reasons, the temperature effect is stronger in\nmultlphase flames.\nThe effect of inlet gas pressure on combustion efficiency is similar to\nthat of inlet temperature.\nIt is seen from figure 14 that, in the case of gas\nphase combustion, an increase of gas pressure from 0.5 to 2.0 kg/cm 2 significantly improves combustion.\nFigure 17 shows the increase of combustion\nefficiency with gas pressure in a multiphase combustion case.\nThe pressure\neffect is negligible at pressures greater than 1.3 kg/cm 2.\nFigure 18 shows the effect of gas-flow velocity on gas-phase combustion\nefficiency.\nAn increase in ga s velocity results in a decrease in combustion\nefficiency because of the reduction of the residence time of the gas flow in\nthe afterburner.\nThe higher the velocity, the lower the efficiency.\nThe\neffect of inlet gas velocity on multlphase combustion efficiency is rather\ncnmnllr_fpdr\n........................ v_Inri_y\n_Inr_\nth_\n\nthe droplet\n\natomization,\n\n_,.__r\xc3\xb7_\n\nvaporization,\n\nnnt,,v_nlv,,,y h_,,__ac_anr_,\n\xc3\xb7\n_,_,,_\n\nand spray\n\ngroup\n\n\xc3\xb7_m_,,.,_\n_h"_\n\ncombustion\n\nalc_\n\nnumber\n\nGc.\n\nCONCLUSIONS\nThe mathematical models and numerical methods for predicting multlphase\nturbulent reacting flows, developed in the authors\' study of spray group combustion phenomena have been successfully extended and applied to the numerical\n16\n\nstudy of flow fields\nand combustion\ncharacteristics\nof a low-bypass-ratio\nturbofan\nafterburner.\nThe computer\ncode developed\nby the authors\nIs versatile\nand effective\nIn thls\nstudy.\nThe technique\nof blocking\noff relevant\ncontrol\nvolumes makes It posslble\nfor a computer code developed\nfor a regular\ngrid\nsystem to be applied\nto arbitrary\nflow domains.\nThe numerical\nanalysls\nreveals\nthat\nthe size of the rectrculation\nzone\nbehind the flameholder\nis about 2.0 to 3.5 times\nIts width.\nThe droplet\nreclrculatlon\nzone Is smaller\nthan the gas reclrculatton\nzone and decreases\nor even\ndisappears\nwith\nan increase\nIn droplet\nsize.\nThe calculation\nalso exposes the existence\nof three\nprincipal\ncombustion\nmodes In multlphase\ncombustion.\nThey are the pre-evaporatlng\nflame mode, the\ndense spray group flame mode, and the dilute\nspray group flame mode.\nStudy of\nflame structure\nIs useful\nto afterburner\ndesign optimization.\nThe results\nreveal\nthe dependence of afterburner\ncombustion\nefficiency\non\nthe fuel-alr\nratio\nand on the operating\nparameters\n(such as the Inlet\ngas temperature,\npressure,\nand velocity).\nAs for multlphase\ncombustion,\nwhich\nprevails\nin turbofan\nafterburners,\nthe spray group combustion\nnumber has a\nsignificant\neffect\non combustion\nefficiency\nand flame structure.\nIt Is concluded that\nafterburners\nshould be designed to work In the optimum spray group\ncombustion\nnumber, which\nis related\nto the fuel-air\nratio\nand Its distribution.\nGenerally\nspeaking,\nthe favorable\ndesign has a pre-evaporatlng\nflame mode or a\ndense group combustion\nmode with\nan Initial\nfuel\ndistribution\ncapable\nof keeplng oxygen-deflclent\nzones as small as possible.\nAlthough\nthe previously\npredicted\nresults\nare In good qualitative\nagreement with\nthe known facts,\naccurate\ndlagnostlc\nmeasurements\nand detailed\nexperimental\nverifications\nare necessary\nto examine the quantitative\nagreement\nand to improve\nthe present\nmodels.\n\nREFERENCES\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\no\n\nChlu, H.H.; and Zhou, Xlaoqlng:\nTurbulent Spray Group Evaporation Effect of inlet gas flow velocity\ncombustion efficiency.\n\n30\n\non gas\n\nN87-20270\nMODELING\n\nTURBULENT,\n\nREACTING\n\nFLOW\n\nRussell W. Claus\nNASA Lewis Research Center\nCleveland,\n\nOhio\n\nThis paper examines several of the approximations\nor models involved in\nthe development\nof a numerical combustor flow code.\nIn the first section, the\nimportance of numerical accuracy is illustrated, and the impact that improvedaccuracy schemes have on slowing convergence is demonstrated.\nSolution algorithms that can speed convergence are discussed and some performance features\nof these algorithms are illustrated.\nA sample calculation displaying the\nimportance of boundary conditions on a three-dlmenslonal\nnumerical prediction\nis presented.\nThe inaccuracy of a current turbu]ence model in highly turbulent (nonequlllbrlum)\nregions is described.\nFinally, the surprisingly good\nperformance of a slx-flux model in descrlblng radiation heat transfer Is dlsplayed.\nIn all the areas examined, continued research is still needed, but\nvaluable engineering tools are available today.\nINTRODUCTION\nThree-dlmenslonal\ncombustor calculatlons involve detailed modeling of\nseveral important physical processes.\nAirflow, chemical reactions, fuel\nsprays, and turbulence are Just a few of the physical processes that must be\ndescribed.\nMany of these processes occur on both a molecular and a macroscopic scale.\nTo exactly describe these processes numerically, one must\nresolve these scales on a computational mesh.\nAnd this is clearly beyond\ncurrent computational\nresources.\nTo make the computational\ntask tractable,\nwe introduced modeling assumptions.\nThese modeling assumptions\nlimit the\ngenerality of the computational\nflow code, but It Is hoped that the dominant\nphysics remain correctly represented.\nModeling assumptions are only the first limit of generality introduced\nwhen developing a combustor flow code.\nA further limit Is introduced by the\nneed to approximate the modeled equations before they are solved numerically.\nThls approximation\nprocess can significantly affect the accuracy of a model\nprediction.\nIn vlew of all these factors, a combustor designer cannot be expected to\nfully embrace a computer model prediction.\nIf a clear distinction\nbetween\nmodeling errors and errors introduced by the numerical solution algorithm cannot be made, then the designer is left wlth a very unreliable computational\ntool.\nThls paper examines some of the compromises made when developing combustor flow codes and how these compromises affect the accuracy of turbulent flow\ncalculations.\nTo assess the balance of the modeling compromises, we compare a\nnumber of sample calculations wlth experimental data.\nThis paper examines the\nflve areas of numerical accuracy, solution algorithms, inlet boundary conditions, turbulence models, and radiative heat transfer.\n\n31\n\nThe NUMERICAL\nACCURACY\nsection focuses on the development of more accurate numerical methods to be used In combustor flow codes. Upwind differencing, which Is currently used in these flow codes, introduces an appreciable\nerror (or numerical diffusion) Into the calculation.\nThis error may be of\nsuch a large magnitude that It obscures the turbulence model used In the calculation.\nA series of calculations are illustrated\nwhich demonstrate the\naccuracy of a variety of differencing schemes.\nAn important\naspect\nof improved\naccuracy\nis the effect\nthat\nthe differencing\nscheme has on the rate of convergence.\nThe improved\naccuracy\nschemes\nall\nappear\nto require\nmore CPU tlme\nto converge.\nFor this\nreason,\nthe next\nsection\ndiscusses\nsolution\nalgorithms.\nSIMPLE (semi-Implicit\npressure-linked\nequations)\nis one of the most widely\nused solution\nalgorithms\nfor solving\nthe\nsteady-state\nform of the Navler-Stokes\nequations.\nAlthough\nthis\nscheme has\nproven\nto be quite effective,\nits\nconvergence\nrate\ncan be improved.\nThis\npaper\nfocuses\non two approaches\nwhich\naccelerate\nconvergence\nby performing\ncorrections\nthat\nimprove\nthe lteratlve\nagreement\nwith\ncontinuity.\nThese\nalternate\nschemes are illustrated\nIn a series\nof calculations.\nA third section\nillustrative example\n\nexamines the importance\nIs displayed.\n\nof inlet\n\nboundary\n\nconditions.\n\nAn\n\nThe fourth section discusses turbulence models and reaction closures.\nHere the more pragmatic approaches to calculating\nturbulent reactions are\nillustrated.\nAn eddy-breakup model and a PDF (probability density function)\nmethod are described and compared.\nFinally, the fifth section examines the accuracy of a radiation heattransfer model.\nA six-flux model of radiative heat transfer Is described.\nThis model provides only a limited geometric description of the radiation\ntransfer process, but a comparison wlth experimental data indicates an encouraging level of agreement.\nNUMERICAL\n\nACCURACY\n\nThe following Is a general form of the dlscretlzatlon using hybrid differencing\nis as follows:\n\nax\nFor upwind,\n\nwith\n\n_\n\n(U_)e - (u_)w\nax\n\n(4)\n\nu > O,\n(u_) e = Ue_ P\n\nand\nl\n(u_)w = _ Uw(_w\n\n+ _,p)\n\nSince upwind differencing is only flrst-order accurate, It can introduce an\nextensive amount of numerical diffusion into the calculation.\nThe virtue of\nthis scheme is that it provides "bounded," stable solutions.\nHigher order\nconvective schemes invariably yield nonphysical oscillations\nin the solution.\nUpwind differencing avoids this, at a considerable\nloss in accuracy.\nTo alleviate this problem, NASA has conducted a program to identify and\nincorporate an improved accuracy differencing scheme into a combustor flow\ncode.\nUnder a portion of this program a variety of differencing\nschemes were\nexamined in several test calculations.\nThe schemes examined included QUICK\n(quadratic upstream interpolation)\nand SUD (skewed upwind differencing).\nQUICK differencing was developed by Leonard (ref. 1).\nThis scheme improves\nthe accuracy of convective differencing by performing an upwind biased quadratic interpolation.\nFor\nu > O,\n\n33\n\n1\n(Urn) e = ue - _mw * 3\n_mp * 3\n_mE /\n\n(S)\n(uq,)w = uw _l\n\n3 w\n\n3p)\n\nwhere grid point locations\nare as noted on figure\n2.\nThis scheme Is secondorder accurate\nand can produce nonphysical\noscillations\ntn the solution.\nSUO\n(skewed upwind differencing)\n(ref.\n2) attains\nhigh accuracy\nby differencing\nin\nan upwind manner along the flow streamlines.\nWhtle\nmaintaining\nthe same formal accuracy\nas upwind differencing,\nthe truncation\nerror\nIn SUO Is smaller.\nFor example:\nFor\n\nu > 0\n\nand\n\nV > O,\n(u_) e = Uem P\n(um) w = Uw(1 - _)m w + Uw=msw\nV = minimum\n\n(6)\n\nof (l,V/2u)\n\nwhere grld point locations are as noted In figure 3. As wlth QUICK, SUD can\nproduce nonphysical oscillations\nin the solution; therefore, a scheme to\n"bound" SUD was also examined.\nThis scheme employs the concept of fluxblending (ref. 3), wherein a bounded flux determined from upwind differencing\nIs blended wlth the unbounded, but more accurate, SUD flux.\nThe maln factor\nIs to blend as little of the lesser accurate scheme while still maintaining\na\nproperly "bounded" solution.\nThls procedure, called BSUDS, starts from an\ninitial, totally skew-dlfferenced\nestimate and blends an upwind flux If the\nsolution Is out of the range of neighboring values.\nIf the solution is In\nrange (i.e., bounded), then no blending Is performed.\nAn illustration of the accuracy of the upwind, QUICK, and SUD schemes Is\nseen In figure 4. This figure displays the results of a slngle-polnt scalartransport calculation made for various flow angles.\nAll schemes agree with an\nexact solution (no error) at a zero flow angle; however, at angles greater\nthan zero, each scheme displays some degree of error relating to numerical\ndiffusion.\nThe error displayed by upwind differencing\nincreases wlth flow\nangle to a maximum at 45 \xc2\xb0. The QUICK scheme displays a similar behavior, but\nthe overall error Is much less.\nThe SUD scheme displays a maximum error\naround 15 \xc2\xb0, but It tends to zero at angles approaching\n45 \xc2\xb0\nBoth QUICK and\nSUD display a much higher level of accuracy than upwind.\nAlthough a scalar transport calculation Is useful for a general examination of some aspects of differencing\nscheme performance, a laminar flow calculation Is a more complete test.\nThe results of a series of laminar flow\ncalculations\nfrom reference 4 are displayed In figure 5. In thls figure,\naxial velocity profiies at a distance of one-half a duct height from the _nlet\nare shown for two different computational\nmeshes.\nIn these calculations,\nthe\nsteepness of the velocity profile indicates accuracy.\nSteep velocity profiles\nare exhibited by QUICK and BSUDS; the upwind profiles exhibit a hlgh degree of\nnumerical diffusion.\nOn the coarse mesh, BSUDS appears to be more accurate\nthan QUICK; on the flne mesh, there Is not much of a distinction\nbetween the\ntwo schemes.\n\n34\n\nAn important\naspect\nof improved\naccuracy\nIs the effect\nthat\nthe differenclng\nscheme has on the rate of convergence.\nThe computational\ntimes\nto\nconverge\nthe system of governing\nequations\nIn the previous\nlaminar\ncalculations\nare shown tn table\nI.\nThe convergence\ntimes\nare ratioed\nto the upwind\nconvergence\ntimes\nto clearly\nillustrate\nthe computational\npenalty\npaid to\nattain\nimproved\naccuracy.\nGenerally,\nthe improved\naccuracy\nschemes required\nfrom 3 to 15 times\nlonger\nto reach a converged\nsolution.\nTo a degree one can\nhope that\nthis\ncomputational\npenalty\ncan be offset\nby using coarse meshes to\nachieve\nthe same overall\nlevel\nof accuracy.\nIn reality,\na relatively\nfine\nmesh Is needed,\neven with\nthe high-accuracy\nschemes.\nIn any case, the need\nfor improved\nsolution\nalgorithms\nfor these more accurate\ndifferencing\nschemes\nIs strongly\nindicated.\nSOLUTION\n\nALGORITHMS\n\nThe previous section demonstrated the need for improved solution algorithms.\nThls section examines the widely used SIMPLE algorithm and two modifications to this scheme.\nThese schemes cover only a small portion of the\nwlde range of methods to accelerate solution convergence.\nVectorlzatlon,\ndlrect-solutlon\nmethods, and multlgrld methods - to llst Just a few - are all\nareas of active research that are certain to yield much greater computational\nbenefits In the near future.\nIn the SIMPLE algorithm, a guessed pressure field is inserted Into the\ndlscretlzed\nmomentum equations to obtain a velocity field.\nThe pressure field\nis corrected by an equation which Is derived through a combination of the continuity and momentum equations.\nThe velocity field Is then updated and is\nused In the solution of the equations for\nk, c, and\n_. The corrected pressure field Is treated as the guessed pressure field, and the procedure Is\nrepeated until a converged solution Is obtained.\n\nfor\n\nThe following velocity\nu at point\ne:\n\ncorrection\n\nequation\n\nis used\n\nIn the SIMPLE\n\nscheme\n\nAeU\'e = n_b AnbUnb + (P_ - P_)A e\nPrimes indicate corrections to old values.\nThe underlined term, which represents the influence of corrected pressures on neighboring velocities,\nis\nneglected In the SIMPLE algorithm.\nThe converged solution Is unchanged by the\nexclusion of thls term slnce, for the steady-state solution, the corrections\ngo to zero.\nNeglecting terms, however, does force the use of low underrelaxatlon factors, which can slow convergence.\nThe SIMPLER\n\nalgorithm\n\nimproves\n\non the SIMPLE\n\nscheme\n\nby including\n\nthe\n\npreviously neglected terms when calculating the pressure field.\nThe calculation sequence starts wlth a guessed velocity field.\nAn equation that solves\nfor the pressure field (using the terms ignored In the SIMPLE scheme) Is calculated from the guessed velocity field.\nThls pressure field is then used to\nsolve the dlscretlzed momentum equations to obtain a velocity field.\nThe\nvelocity field Is corrected In a manner similar to the SIMPLE velocity correction.\nThls velocity field Is then treated as the guessed velocity field, and\nthe iteration procedure is repeated until convergence Is reached.\n\n35\n\nBecause additional equations are solved, each iteration through the\nSIMPLER routine involves more computational\ntime than an iteration step through\nSIMPLE.\nHowever, higher underrelaxatlon\nfactors can be applied in the SIMPLER\nroutine, thereby accelerating convergence.\nThe PISO scheme also takes into account the terms neglected in the SIMPLE\ncode, but in a different manner.\nThe PISO routine mimics the SIMPLE approach\nuntil the end of the first iteration.\nAt this point, the PISO scheme employs\nan equation containing the neglected terms to correct the pressure and velocIty field to more closely agree with continuity.\nAgain, this procedure is\nrepeated until the solution converges.\nIn this manner, the PISO code allows\nfor higher underrelaxation\nfactors to accelerate convergence.\nThe performance of these various solution schemes is displayed in\nfigure 6. As an example, the convergence times for a 38-by-38 grid point\ncalculation are plotted as a function of underrelaxation\nfactor (fig. 6(a)).\nWhen a reasonably large underrelaxatlon\nfactor is used, SIMPLER or PISO converge about twice as fast as SIMPLE.\nIn addition, SIMPLER and PISO converge\nover a larger range of underrelaxatlon\nfactors than SIMPLE.\nFrom an engineering standpoint, improving the "robustness" of a computational\nscheme is often\nJust as important as accelerating convergence.\nThe computational\nbenefit of\nusing SIMPLER and PISO for flne-mesh calculations\nis displayed in figure 6(b).\nThe greater the number of mesh points used in the calculation,\nthe greater the\nbenefit of SIMPLER or PISO over SIMPLE.\nFor example, a calculation\nof approximately 3300 grid points converges three times faster using PISO or SIMPLER\nthan it does using SIMPLE.\nThis is a savings of about 600 CPU seconds.\nThe\n1440 grid points calculation demonstrated a savings of only about lO0 CPU\nseconds.\nINLET\n\nBOUNDARY\n\nCONDITIONS\n\nIn any calculation of a complex, three-dlmenslonal\nturbulent flow, the\nboundary conditions that are needed in the calculation are frequently unknown\nor unmeasured.\nThe use of inappropriate\nvalues at the computational\nboundary\ncan sometimes be the main limit to the calculatlon\'s\npredictive capability.\nThis error can sometimes be more important than numerical accuracy or turbulence model considerations.\nFigure 7 shows an example of a three-dlmenslonal\nJet-ln-crossflow\ncalculation using alternate boundary conditions.\nIn one\ncalculation, the Jet orifice flow was specified as having a uniform plug flow\nat a position two Jet diameters upstream of the orifice outlet.\nThis allowed\nthe flow to distort as it exited the orifice outlet.\nThe second calculation\nspecified a uniform plug flow at the orifice outlet.\nThe resulting axial\nvelocity profiles are compared with experimental data in figure B. Somewhat\nsurprisingly,\nthe uniform boundary condition at the orifice compared more\nfavorably with experimental data than the theoretically more correct distorted\nprofile.\nThis should not be interpreted as an endorsement of the use of\nunlform-plug-flow\nboundary conditions for these types of flows.\nIndeed, there\nis some indication of experimental error (ref. 5).\nThis example is meant only\nto illustrate that unmeasured or unknown boundary conditions can significantly\naffect a numerical calculation.\nTURBULENCE\n\nMODELS\n\nAlthough a great deal of progress is being made in solving the threedimensional,\ntlme-dependent Navler-Stokes\nequations in large-eddy or direct\n36\n\nnumerical\nsimulations,\npractical\nthe introduction\nof some form of\n\nengineering\nturbulence\n\ncalculations\nmodeling.\n\ncurrently\nThese models\n\nrequire\nare based\n\non either\nReynolds or Favre averaging\nof the exact Navter-Stokes\nequations,\nreducing\nthe unsteady form of these\nequations\nto an averaged\nform.\nCurrently,\nthe most widely\nused turbulence\nmodel Is the two equation,\nk-c,\nclosure.\nThis\nmodel relates\nthe Reynolds stresses\nto a turbulent\nviscosity\nthrough\nBousslnesq\'s\neddy-viscosity\nconcept:\n\npuiu\n\n: P"T aTj+ axt/\n\nwhere\nv T = Cuk2/c.\nThe turbulent\nviscosity\nIs related\nenergy\nk\nand the dissipation\nrate\nc of the turbulence.\nequations\nare then solved for\nk\nand c:\n\nUi\n\na xl\n\n-\n\naxl\\_k--\n\n_\'_ IJ\n\n+ VTta-_\n\n(8)\n\n7 61Jk\nto\n\n\xc3\xb7 axlj_-\n\nthe kinetic\nThe transport\n\ni:\n\n(9)\nUI\nWhere\n\nC\n\naxt\n\n-\n\naXl _- l-)c_\'_\n\n.i- Col _. lITta-._-_ + _-\'_\'1) ax,\'l\n\n= o.og, Col = 1.44, Cc2 = 1.92, _k = 1.O,\n\n(I12)(_ \'2 + _,2 + _,2)\n\nand\n\no\n\n= 1.3, o\n\n= 0.9,\n\nk =\n\nc = C k3/211T .\n\nThe model constants typically employed are those recommended in\nreference 6. employed\nIn the design of combustion devices.\nCertainly a great deal of research is\nneeded before one can expect quantitative predictive accuracy, and It seems\nlikely that some hardware problems wlll only be resolved through development\ntesting.\nThe best computer program wlll never replace the designer\'s innovative mind, but computer predictions can be used to extend the designer\'s productivity.\nNew designs can be examined much more rapidly on the computer than\nin hardware testing.\nDevelopment costs can be reduced.\nThe promise of thls\ncomputer-based\ndesign methodology\nIs so great that these numerical models wlll\nbe used despite their deficiencies.\nDesigners should not and probably will\n\n39\n\nnot abandon empirical design\nmodels in the design process\n\ntools, but the cautious adoption of numerical\nIs a trend which can reap important benefits.\nREFERENCES\n\nl. Radiative heat transfer\n\n46\n\ntest calculation.\n\nN87-20271\nEXPERIMENTAL\n\nAND ANALYTICAL\n\nCHARACTERISTICS\n\nALONG\n\nINVESTIGATION\nA RADIAL\n\nPRESSURE-SWIRL\n\nBelJlng\n\nOF THE VARIATION\n\nDISTANCE\n\nDOWNSTREAM\n\nOF SPRAY\n\nOF A\n\nATOMIZER\n\nJ.S. Chin, W.M. LI, and X.F. Wang\nInstitute of Aeronautics and Astronautics\nBelJlng, People\'s Republic of China\n\nThe variation of spray characteristics\nalong a radial distance downstream\nof a pressure-swlrl\natomizer was measured by laser llght-scatterlng\ntechnology.\nAn analytical model was developed to predict the variation of spray characteristics along the radial distance.\nA comparison of the predicted and experimental data showed excellent agreement.\nTherefore, the spray model proposed,\nalthough relatively simple, is correct and can be used, with some expansion\nand modification\nof the prepared model, to predict more complicated spray\nsystems.\n\nINTRODUCTION\nThe characteristics\nof the spray formed by an atomizer in a llquld-fueled\ncombustion device are extremely important to the performance,\nstability, and\npollutant formation of the combustor.\nThe spray characteristics\nshould be\nmeasured accurately, and a model should be developed to predict the variation\nof spray characteristics,\ndroplet trajectory, spray dispersion, and evaporation history.\nIt is also important to understand the influence of various\nfactors on the measurement of spray characteristics.\nA comparison between\npredicted and measured spray characteristics\nprovides useful insight into\nspray combustion.\nSuch research has a slgnlflcant effect on engineering\napplications and on the fundamental understanding\nof spray combustion.\nThe authors analyzed the variation of spray characteristics\nalong the\naxial distance downstream of a pressure-swirl\natomizer.\nThe effect of spray\nevaporation on the variation of spray characteristics\nis discussed in\nreference 1 and was presented by J.S. Chin and J.Y. Zhu at this symposium.\n(Paper, entitled The Interdependence\nof Spray Characteristics\nand Evaporation\nHistory of Fuel Sprays in High Temperature Airflows, was unavailable for\nprinting at the time of publication.)\nThe influence of downstream distance on\nthe spray characteristics\nof pressure-swirl\natomizers is caused by the effects\nof spray dispersion and drop acceleration or deceleration.\nThis condltlon is\nanalyzed in reference 2. These analyses have been partially substantiated\nby\nsome experimental data.\nUntil now there have been few experimental data of\nthe variation of spray characteristics\nalong the radial distance downstream of\nthe pressure-swlrl\natomizer.\nLee Dodge published his measurement\nresults as\nshown in figure 1 (ref. 3).\nHe shows that both the Sauter mean diameter SMD\nand drop-slze distribution parameter\nN\nincrease with radial distance.\nThere\nis no analysis on this aspect available at the present time.\nWe are interested in comparing the experimental and predicted values of\nthe variation of spray characterlstlcs\nalong the radial distance.\nTherefore,\nwe carefully measured this variation in a well-deflned\nexperimental condition,\n"developed an analytical model to predict the variation, and then compared the\n47\n\nexperimental and predicted\n\ndata.\nThese comparisons promote our understanding\nof spray dispersion and the factors influencing drop-slze measurement.\nAfter\nwe have predicted the simple spray system, we will be able to expand and\nmodify the proposed spray model to extend its capability to more complicated\nspray systems.\nCao and Chin proposed\n\na flat-fan\n\nspray model\n\nfor the fuel distribution\n\ndownstream of a plain orifice injector under cross airflow (ref. 4).\nThe\nmodel was validated by fuel distribution measured by gas analysis and drop\nsize.\nIt is obvious that, for the validation of a given spray model, the\nspray characteristics measurement\nis reliably accurate and much simpler than\nthe fuel distribution measurement.\nIt is this author\'s intention to validate\na spray model by measuring the spray characteristics\nalong the radial distance.\nThe same spray model has also been validated by measuring the spray characteristics along different\nB\nangles (as shown in fig. 5).\n\nTEST APPARATUS,\n\nATOMIZER,\n\nAND PARTICLE\n\nSIZER\n\nThe test section was a rectangular chamber with transparent windows for\noptical drop-slze measurement.\nThe test apparatus included an air system and\na fuel system, as shown in figure 2.\nThe atomizer tested (fig. 3) was a pressure-swlrl\natomizer taken from an\nexisting aircraft gas-turblne engine.\nThe atomizer had a pilot-fuel flow and\na main-fuel flow.\nIn this test we used the pilot-fuel passage.\nBecause the\nflow rate is relatively lower with pilot-fuel flow, the obscuratlon was In the\nright range.\nA Malvern drop-slze analyzer model 2200 was used.\nThe principle of the\nparticle slzer is shown in figure 4. Chin and J.Y. Zhu in the unpublished paper cited previously), and it is\nnot difficult to take into consideration\nthe effect of droplet evaporation.\nAfter this model\nthe fuel distribution\n\nhas been validated, it is relatively simple to predict\ndownstream of a pressure-swirl\natomizer (ref. 6).\n\nOne interesting point that was noticed from the experiments and the analysis was that the larger droplets are always at the spray edge.\nBecause the\nignition spark plug is also always positioned close to the spray edge, the\nspark might meet the larger droplets, which would harm the ignition.\nWe measured the variation of spray characteristics\nalong the radial distance was by laser light-scatterlng\ntechnology.\nAnd we found that the prediction based on the proposed spray model was in excellent agreement with the\nexperimental data.\n\nREFERENCES\nl\n\n\xc2\xb0\n\n.\n\nJ.S. Taclna\nLewis Research Center\nCleveland, Ohio\n\nAn experimental program to characterize the spray from candidate nozzles\nfor Iclng-cloud\nsimulation is discussed.\nOne candidate nozzle, which is currently used for icing research, has been characterized\nfor flow and drop size.\nThe medlan-volume\ndiameter (MVD) from this alr-asslst nozzle is compared with\ncorrelations\nin the literature.\nThe new experimental\nspray facility is discussed, and the drop-slze instruments are discussed in detail.\nSince there is\nno absolute standard for drop-slze measurements and there are other limitations, such as drop-slze range and velocity range, several instruments are used\nand the results are compared.\nThe drop-slze instruments used are the Malvern\n2600, the NASA Lewis Scanning Radiometer, the Phase/Doppler\nSpray Analyzer\n(P/DSA), a Forward-Scatterlng\nSpectrometer Probe (FSSP), an Optical Array Probe\n(OAP), and a limited amount of photography.\nThe known capabilities and llmltations of the drop-slze instruments are compared.\nA two-phase model was developed at Pennsylvania State University.\nThe\nmodel uses the\nk-c model of turbulence in the contlnous phase.\nThree methods\nfor treating the discrete phase are used:\n(1) a locally homogenous flow (LHF)\nmodel, (2) a deterministic\nseparated flow (DSF) model, and (3) a stochastic\nseparated flow (SSF) model.\nIn the LHF model both phases have the same velocity and temperature at each point.\nThe DSF model provides Interphase transport\nbut ignores the effects of turbulent fluctuations.\nIn the SSF model the drops\ninteract with turbulent eddies whose properties are determined by the\nk-c\nturbulence model.\nThe two-phase flow model has been extended to include the\neffects of evaporation and combustion.\nModel development has been accompanied\nby an experimental program to provide data for verification.\nPresently, the\nmodel has been further extended at NASA Lewis to include the effects of\nswirling flow, and a partlcle-laden\nswirling flow experiment\nbeing performed to provide verification data.\n\nis currently\n\nINTRODUCTION\nSprays and two-phase flow are of interest in many fields - for example,\ngas turbine combustion, agricultural crop spraying, paint spraying, fire protection, and drying.\nThis paper describes the use of sprays to simulate icing\nclouds for the Altitude Wind Tunnel Icing Research Program.\nIclng-cloud simulation and gas turbine combustion research have common interests in the method\nof injection, the characterization\nof drop size and of droplet velocities, and\nthe development of analytical models for droplet trajectories\nand droplet\nvaporization.\nThis paper discusses injector or spray-nozzle drop-slze characterization, an experimental test facility for injector characterization,\ndropsize measurement\ntechniques, and analytical models for trajectory calculations.\n\nIN3ECTOR\n\nCHARACTERIZATION\n\nThe injector, or spray nozzle, used currently\nat the NASA Lewis Research Center is an alr-asslst\n63\n\nfor Iclng-cloud\nsimulation\ninjector (fig. l).\nIt is\n\nvery similar\n\nto the injector used by Nuklyama and Tanasawa (ref. 1).\nThe water\nflows through a center tube (0.635-mm dlam) and has a concentric annular passage (9.35-mm dlam) for alr-asslst flow.\nThe water and the air exit through a\n3.175-mm orifice.\nThe advantage of an alr-asslst\ninjector for icing research\nand other applications is that, within limits, there Is nearly independent\ncontrol of water flowrate and drop size.\nThe water flow Is controlled by the\ndifference between the air pressure and water pressure, and the drop slze Is\ncontrolled by the alr pressure only.\nDrop-slze measurements using this injector were made for us at Arnold\nEngineering Development Center by J.L. Hunt.\nThe drop-slze data shown In\nfigure 2 are plotted as medlan-volume\ndiameter in micrometers.\nThe medianvolume diameter\nMVD\nis defined as the drop size where 50 percent of the total\ndroplet volume is contained in droplets less than that diameter, and 50 percent\nis contained In droplets greater than that diameter.\nThe MVD is plotted as a\nfunction of the difference of water and alr-asslst pressure wlth the airassist pressure as a parameter.\nThis method of correlating the data is very useful for setting the operating conditions in the wind tunnel.\nHowever, the drop-slze data could be\npresented in a more fundamental manner as a function of the water to air mass\nratio.\nOther factors that appear in correlations\nIn the literature,\nsuch as\nviscosity, surface tension, and relative velocity between the air and water,\nare constant or nearly constant for these data.\nThe relative velocity Is\nnearly constant, even though the flowrates are changing.\nThe reason for this\nIs, first, that the alr Is being injected at high pressure into a low-pressure\nambient environment and thus has sonic, or nearly sonic, velocity.\nSecond, the\nwater pressure is relatively low so that the water has a relatively small\nvelocity compared to the sonic velocity of the air.\nThus, the difference In\nthe velocities or the relative velocity is constant.\nThese data were compared to values from various correlations.\nThese comparisons are shown in figure 3 for one alr-asslst pressure.\nThe injectors used\nin the references were similar to the NASA Lewis Research Center injector.\nHowever, the drop-slze measurement\ntechniques were different.\nThe NuklyamaTanasawa (ref. l) drop-slze correlation was made from measurements\nof water\ndroplets on photographs.\nThe Lorenzetto-Lefebvre\ncorrelation\n(ref. 2) was made\nfrom llght-scatterlng\nmeasurements\nof water droplets.\nWlgg (ref. 3) used\nmeasurements\nfrom molten wax sprays with swirl.\nAs you can see in the figure,\nthe results of the correlations\nhave a wide range of values, some higher and\nsome lower than the data taken at Arnold.\nPreliminary data taken at NASA\nLewis seem to be closest to the data of Nuklyama-Tanasawa.\nPreliminary\ndata taken With the NASA Lewis injector at Carnegle-Mellon\nUniversity are lower\nthan the Arnold data.\nThere needs to be much more work on drop-slze measurements and correlations before drop sizes can be accurately predicted.\n\nTEST\n\nFACILITY\n\nThe objectives of our experimental program are to characterize a variety\nof candidate most important factor In making drop-slze measurements\nis the dropslze measuring instrument.\nAs you have seen from the figure comparing the\ndrop-slze data correlations,\nthere must be large differences\nin the data on\nwhich they are based.\nOne of the major problems is that there Is no standard\nto which to compare the drop-slze measurements,\nespecially In a spray.\nSince\nthere are other limitations beside the lack of a standard such as drop-slze\nrange and velocity range, In our program we use five different drop-slze\ninstruments and compare the results.\nThe instruments are a Malvern 2600, a\nNASA Lewis Scattered-Llght\nScanner, a Phase/Doppler\nSpray Analyzer, a ForwardScattering Spectrometer Probe, and an Optical Array Probe.\nAnd a limited\n\n65\n\namount of photography is used for qualitative\nand a discussion of each of these instruments\n\nanalysis.\nfollows.\n\nA qualitative\n\nanalysis\n\nThe Malvern 2600 is a commonly used drop-slze instrument (fig. 5).\nLewis\'\nHSD version of this instrument, which provides a faster snapshot of the data,\nis described in reference 4. (The inserts in figure 5 describe the principle\nof operation.)\nThis instrument measures the angle that the light is diffracted\nby the drops.\nThe bigger drops diffract the light at a small angle and the\nsmaller drops diffract the light at a larger angle.\nThe detector is a series\nof concentric rings that determine the angle of the scattered light.\nThe drop\nsize is calculated from the scattered light angle.\nThe insert also shows that,\nno matter where the droplet is in the llne of sight, the light that is scattered at the same angle always falls on the same detector ring.\nAlthough there is no standard for determining drop sizes, we use a\nHirleman reticle for calibration purposes.\nThe Hirleman reticle (fig. 6) consists of a glass substrate on which chrome disks are photographically\ndeposited.\nThe chrome disks are sized to represent a Rosln-Rammler distribution\nwith an x-bar of 50 and an n value of 3. One problem we have found using\nthe reticle with the Malvern 2600 is that there is a correction for anomalous\ndiffraction.\nThe Malvern calculations are based primarily on Fraunfaufer diffraction theory.\nHowever, for small drop sizes (below a diameter of 5 pm)\nthree-dimensional\neffects are considered; these effects are lumped under the\nterm anomalous diffraction.\nThus, the problem is that the reticle has twodimensional\ndisks and not three-dimenslonal\nspheres.\nWe are currently studying\nthe feaslblllty of modifying the Malvern software to ignore the anomalous diffraction calculation when using the reticle.\nIf this does not prove to be\npractical, we will still use the reticle for relative comparisons.\nWe also plan to add an additional lens system to the Malvern to be able\nto measure an MVD of 5 pm across the 30-cm instrument section.\nVerification\nof the correctness of the additional lens system will be done by taking measurements of the retlcle with and without the additional lens system.\nThe second instrument, the Scattered-Light\nScanner, is also based on the\ndiffraction\nprinciple.\nThis is a revised version of the NASA Lewis Scanning\nRadiometer (described in ref. 5), which was developed by Don Buchele and used\nin many research projects by Robert Ingebo.\nFigures 7 to 9 explain the operation of the instrument.\nFigure 7 shows the reflection, refraction, and dlffractlon\nof a laser\nlight beam passing through a drop.\nSince calculations are based on the diffraction of light through the drop, the wlde-angle diffraction\nis ignored.\nThere is narrow-angle diffracted light from the envelope (or aperture) of the\nlaser beam that must also be ignored.\nF!gur_ R(a) It a plot of light intensity as a function of the llght-scatter angle.\nFigure 8(b) shows the product of the light intensity and the llghtscatter angle as a function of the light-scatter\nangle.\nIt shows that the\nmeasurement\ninterval extends from where the intensity of the near envelope\ndiffraction\nis negligible to a value of the light intensity which is still high\nenough to be meaningful.\nA scanner is used to measure the intensity of the\nscattered Pennsylvania\nState\nUniversity\nby Professor\nFaeth and his graduate\nstudents\nand associates\n(refs.\n8 to 10).\nFtgure\n17 shows the Important\nInteractions\nof the drop with\nIts environment.\nThese Interactions\nare:\nthe drop]et\ndrag that\nresults\ntn a source term tn the\ncontinuous\nflow equation,\nrandom deflections\nof the drops by turbulent\neddtes,\nand turbulence\nmodulation\nof the continuous\nphase by the extraction\nof turbulent\nenergy by the droplet\ndrag.\nActually,\nthree\nmodels\nof Increasing\ncomplextty\nwere developed.\nThey are a locally\nhomogenous flow model,\na determlntstlc\nseparated-flow\nmodel,\nand a stochastic\nseparated-flow\nmodel.\nThe locally\nhomogenous flow model ts the simplest\nmodel (ftg.\n18).\nIt\nuses the\nk-c\nmodel to calculate\nthe continuous-phase\nflow characteristics.\nIt then assumes that\nthe particles\nhave the same velocity\nas the continuous\nphase and treats\nthe\nk-c\nequations\nas a varying-density\nflow.\nThis model Is\ncorrect\nfor small drops that\nfollow\nthe continuous\nphase flow.\nAn advantage\nof the model ts that\nthe calculation\nIs no more complex for a two-phase\nflow\nthan for a single-phase\nflow.\nIt Is also useful\nwhen the lntttal\nconditions\nof the spray,\nsuch as drop size or spray angle,\nare not available\nsince they\nare not needed tn the calculation.\nThe second model Is the deterministic\nseparated\nflow (DSF) model\n(fig.\n19).\nIt also uses the\nk-c\nmodel to calculate\nthe gas or continuousphase flowfleld.\nThe parttcle\nor droplet\ndistribution\nIs dtvtded\ntnto\na number\nof classes\nwtth\na representative\nnumber of droplets\ntn each class.\nThen, for\neach drop,\nthe trajectory\nts calculated.\nThe trajectory\ncalculation\nts based\non the mean continuous-phase\nflow properties.\nDispersion\ndue to turbulence\nIs\nIgnored.\nThts Is the most wtdely\nused approximation\nIn current\nmodels of combusting\nsprays.\nOther assumptions\nare that\nthe drops are spherical\nand that\nthe spray Is dilute\nwtth\nno collisions.\nOne feature\nof this\nmodel ts the partlcle-ln-cell\napproach.\nAs a particle\ngoes through\na cell,\nthe drag momentum ts coupled wtth\nthe continuous-phase\nmomentum.\nThe net change In momentum Is calculated\nfor each drop class\nas It\npasses through\na computational\ncell\nas shown tn figUre\n20.\nThen the gas or\ncontinuous-phase\nflow equatton\nIs calculated\nwith\nthe change tn momentum as a\nsource term.\nThe last,\nand most complex,\nmodel Is the stochastic\nseparated\nflow (SSF)\nmodel (fig.\n21).\nIn this\nmodel,\nthe conttnuous_phaSe\nturbulence\ndoes Interact\nwith\nthe drop or particle.\nAgain the gas-phase\ncalculation\nIs made using the\nk-c\nmodel.\nTo make the Lagraglan\ndrop-trajectory\ncalculation\nwe assume the\ndrop Interacts\nwith\nan eddy.\nThe properties\nof the eddy have a distribution\nbased on a probability\ndensity\nfunctton\nwith\na standard\ndeviation\ndependence\non k.\nFrom the distribution,\nthe properties\nof the eddy are found by using a\nMonte Carlo technique.\nThe Interaction\nts shown tn figure\n22.\nThe drop\nInteracts\nwtth the eddy etther\nfor the 11fetlme\nof the eddy or the time tt\ntakes\nthe particle\nto traverse\nthe eddy.\nThe stze of the eddy Is based on the\ndissipation\nlength.\nThe number of calculations\nare much greater\nfor thts\n\n69\n\nmethod than for the deterministic\nseparated flow (DSF) model.\nWhile for the\nDSF model approximately 600 drops are sufficient for an accurate calculation,\nfor the stochastic separated flow model about 6000 drops are needed for an\naccurate calculation.\nAn experimental study was also done to complement the analytical modeling\neffort.\nFrom a large amount of data, four plots were chosen as representlve.\nFigure 23 is a plot of the mean axial velocities of the drops as a function of\nthe drop diameter at different axial locations.\nAn alr-asslst injector wlth\nan exlt-orlfice diameter of l.lg4 mm was used.\nThe initial conditions were\ntaken at an\nx/d of 50, where\nx Is the axial distance and\nd\nis the Injector-orifice diameter.\nThls is a distance of about 55 mm.\nThis was sufficient\ndistance for the spray to be well formed for most conditions.\nHowever, there\nwere some conditions where ligaments were observed.\nThe drop-slze and velocity measurements were made using double-flash\nphotography.\nTwo other methods were used to measure drop size.\nOne was slide\nImpactlon in which a slide coated with magnesium oxide was used.\nThe second\nalternate method was to measure drop size with a Malvern.\nThe Malvern measurement was used mainly to monitor the spray to determine whether the spray\nvaried from day to day.\nThe data were compared wlth the calculations from the stochastic separated\nflow model and the deterministic\nseparated flow model.\nThe calculations agree\nwell, with the data from the stochastic separated flow model agreeing the best.\nFor all the data plots, the stochastic separated flow model agreed well with\nthe data, while the deterministic\nseparated flow model and the locally homogenous model agreed well for some conditions and not so well for other conditions.\nFigure 24 shows the radial variation of mean liquid flux\nG.\nFor this\ncase, the SMD at an\nx/d of 50 was 30.\nAgain the calculations wlth the\nstochastic separated flow model agree well with the data for all conditions,\nwhile the other two models do not agree well for all conditions.\nIt Is interesting to note that the locally homogenous model underpredlcts\nthe data.\nFor\nall the previous work In which the locally homogenous model was calibrated with\nsolld-particle-laden\nflow, the model overpredlcted\nthe development of the\nspray.\nFigure 25 shows the variation of the Sauter mean diameter with the dlstance along the axis.\nFigure 25(a) is for a nonevaporatlng\noll spray and\nfigure 25(b) is for an evaporating Freon ll spray.\nCase 1 had an initial SMD\nof 30 at an\nx/d of 50 and case 2 had an initial SMD of 87.\nFor the nonevaporating case, the mean diameter increases after an x/d\nof lO0 because of the\ngreater dispersion of the small drops.\nThe stochastic separated flow model\ndoes better at predicting the data because it considers the dispersion due to\nturbulence.\nThe variation of SMO along the axis for an evaporating\nspray Is shown In\nfigure 25(b).\nFor an evaporating spray, there are competing effects:\nevaporation reduces the mean diameter, and greater dispersion of the small drops\nincreases the mean diameter along the axis.\nThe stochastic separated flow\nmodel and deterministic separated flow model do a good Job of predicting the\ntrends even if they do not exactly duplicate the data.\n\n70\n\nThe model development\nIs continuing\nat the NASA Lewis Research experiment.\n\nN87-20273\nCOMBUSIION\n\nRESEARCH\n\nJiangyou\n\nACIIVIIlES\n\nAT THE GAS TURBINE\n\nRESEARCH\n\nShao Zhongpu\nGas Turbine Research Institute\nCounty of Sichuan, People\'s Republic\n\nINSIlIUIE\n\nof China\n\nThe Gas Turbine Research Institute (GTRI) is responsible mainly for basic\nresearch in aeronautical propulsion.\nThe combustion research activities primarily cover the following areas:\nannular diffuser for the turbofan augmentor,\ncombustor ignition performance, combustor airflow distribution,\nfuel injectors,\nvaporizer fuel injector, and airblast atomizer.\n\nRESEARCH\n\nON THE ANNULAR\n\nDIFFUSER\n\nFOR THE TURBOFAN\n\nAUGMENIOR\n\nIt is well known that the inlet velocity profile has a very strong influence on diffuser performance.\nThe inlet velocity and temperature profiles are\nnot uniform:\nboth change with flight condition.\nThe diffuser enlarges the\ninlet velocity distortion; that is, the nonunlformlty\nof the velocity increases\nwith the diffusion process occurring in the diffuser.\nThe nonuniform inlet\nvelocity profile encourages the alrstream to separate from the diffuser wall\nor inner cone, induces flame stabilization in the separated flow region, and\ncauses augmentor components to burn or to induce combustion instability,\nlo\nobtain a desirable fuel-alr distribution,\nit is necessary to understand the\nvelocity and temperature profiles in the augmentor.\nIn other words, since the\nvelocity and temperature profiles at the exit of the diffuser must be known\nsome experimental\nresearch was carried out on the annular diffuser of the turbofan augmentor.\nThe test model and the instrumentation\nlocations\nThe test was run at the following Because it is important for combustion engineers to understand\nthe relation between ignition performance and combustor inlet parameters, we\nstudied the effect of inlet parameters on the ignition performance of combustors with different fuel-injectlon\nsystems.\nThis test was performed on the hlgh-altltude\nignition test rig shown in\nfigure 7. Three combustor configurations\nwere used.\nThe first configuration\nwas a gas turbine combustor with a pressure swirl atomizer.\n(This combustor\nis similar to a JT-3D engine combustor with one flame tube.)\nThe second configuration was a one-quarter\nsector of a full annular combustor with three\nT-shaped vaporizers, as shown in figure 8. The third configuration\nwas similar\nto the second one - the only difference being that the three vaporizers had\nbeen replaced by three alrblast atomizers.\nA hlgh-energy ignition plug was used for every configuration.\nThe stored\nenergy of the ignition system was 20 J, 12 J, and 8 J, respectively.\nThe ignition procedures were repeated three times under every test condition.\nIf all\nthree ignitions were successful, then each was Judged a successful ignition at\nits specific condition.\nWe drew the following\n\nconclusions\n\nfrom\n\nthis experiment:\n\n(1) The inlet pressure has a significant effect on the ignition performance of the combustorso\nFigure 9 shows the optimum fuel-air ratio as a func\ntlon of inlet pressure for the three configurations.\nFrom figure 9 we can see\nthe effect of the inlet air pressure on ignition performance of the combustors,\nespecially at low pressure.\n(2) The inlet air temperature has a strong effect on the ignition performance of the combustors.\nThe effects of the inlet air temperature\non maximum\nignitable reference velocity and on optimum fuel-alr ratio for the three configurations are shown in figures lO and II. As the inlet air temperature\ndecreases, the maximum ignitable reference velocity decreases.\nAt higher inlet\n90\n\nair temperatures,\nthe effect\nof the Inlet\natr temperature\non the optimum\nable fuel-air\nratio\nIs very weak; however,\nat lower\ninlet\nalr\ntemperatures\neffect\nis very strong,\nespecially\nfor the vaporizer\ncombustor.\n\nsure\n\nFigures\n12(a),\nand temperature\n\n(b),\nand (c)\non ignition\n\nEXPERIMENIAL\n\nshow the combined\neffects\nperformance\nfor the three\n\nSTUDY ON AIRFLOW\n\nDISTRIBUTION\n\nof inlet\nair\nconflgurations.\n\nignitthe\n\npres-\n\nOF THE COMBUSIOR\n\nThe airflow distribution\ncharacteristics of a combustor directly affect\nthe performance of the combustor such as combustion efficiency,\nrange of stable operation, and temperature profile at the combustor exit.\nA 90 \xc2\xb0 segment\nof the annular combustor was used for this study.\nThe test assembly Is shown\nin figure 13.\nThe goals\n\nof the study were\n\nas follows:\n\n(1) To determine the effect of Mach number at the combustor inlet on the\nairflow distribution\nat an alr temperature of 291 K\n(2) To compare the changes of airflow distributions\nwlth and without\nburning in the combustor\nFigure 13 shows the eight instrumentation\nsections on the test assembly.\nThe flow rate\nGhl\nthrough the holes on the wall of the flame tube can be\ncalculated as follows:\n\nGhl = CdlAhl\n\n2_\n\nAP\n\nwhere\nCdl\nAhl\n\nairflow\narea\n\ndischarge\n\ncoefficient\n\nof the holes\n\nof the holes\n\nY\n\nspecific\n\ngravity\n\nAp\n\npressure\n\ndrop through\n\nacceleration\n\nof alr\nthe holes,\n\nof gravity\n\nThe flow rate (In percent)\ntotal is\n\nthrough\n\neach row of holes\n\nGi = lO0\n\nThe relative\n\narea\n\n= Pal -
