Zeleznlk\nLewis Research Center\nCleveland, Ohio\n\nModeling fluid phase phenomena blends the conservation equations of continuum mechanics with the property equations of thermodynamics.\nThe thermodynamic contribution becomes especially important when the phenomena involve\nchemical reactions as they do in combustion systems.\nThe successful study of\ncombustion processes requires\n(1) the availability\nof accurate thermodynamic\nproperties for both the reactants and the products of reaction and (2) the\ncomputational\ncapabilities to use the properties.\nA discussion is given of\nsome aspects of the problem of estimating accurate thermodynamic\nproperties\nboth for reactants and products of reaction.\nAlso, some examples of the use\nof thermodynamic\nproperties for modeling chemically reacting systems are presented.\nThese examples include one-dlmenslonal\nflow systems and the internal\ncombustion engine.\n\nINTRODUCT ION\nModeling fluid phase phenomena always requires a combination of the conservation equations of continuum mechanics with the property functions of\nthermodynamics.\nFor some applications it is possible to idealize the model so\ndrastically\nthat the continuum equations virtually disappear and the computation becomes wholly thermodynamic.\nIn other situations the thermodynamic\nproperties are so highly idealized that the computation deals solely with the\ncontinuum equations.\nConsequently,\nthermodynamics and continuum mechanics are\noften viewed as independent disciplines.\nHowever, the complementary\nnature of\nthe two disciplines\nis manifested in attempts to generate realistic models of\nphysical systems in which combustion occurs.\nThe roles of thermodynamics\nand the conservation equations are easily discerned by examining the relevant equations.\nThe conservation equations\n(refs. l and 2) for mass, species, energy, and momentum are shown in\nequation (1), where the summation convention is used for an index repeated as\na subscript and a superscript:\na__m\n6m\nk\nat \xc3\xb7 Vk (mvk) = 0 = _-_ \xc3\xb7 mvkv\n\na(mnx)\nat\n\n\xc3\xb7 vk(mn_vk\n\n+ vk(muv k) = - Vk( qk + _), k \xc3\xb7 TkJvj vk - m _\na_ - vkfk\nd})\n\nat\n\n6v k\nm _\n:\n\nT\n\nkj\n\n\xc3\xb7 d_) = R_\n\n:\n\n-\n\nFk\n\n= -\n\npgkJ\n\n\xc3\xb7\n\nmgklvl_\n\n+\n\nfk\n\n6u\n= m _-_\n\n(1)\n\n\xc3\xb7 Vj_ kj\n\n8kJ\nll3\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n_\n\n__\'l_ _,Cr_\n\nIn these equations,\n\nm\n\nis the mass density,\n\nu\n\nthe internal\n\nenergy per unit\nk\nmass, nx the moles per unit mass of species\nx, v\nthe velocity, q\nthe\nk\nheat flux, _\nthe chemical potential per mole of species\nx, d_\nthe dlffuk\n\nsive flux of species\nX, and\nRx\nthe volumetric rate of production of species\nk.\nIn the momentum equation, gkl\nis the metric tensor for three-dlmenslonal\nspace, Tkj\nis the stress tensor, _\nis the potential energy, and\nfk represents\nall volumetric forces over and above those produced by the stress and the\npotential energy.\nThe stress tensor is composed of a contribution from the\npressure\np and the dissipative stresses.\nThe symbol\nt represents time,\nvk is the covarlant derivative with respect to the spatial coordinates, and\nb/at = a/at \xe2\x80\xa2 vkvk is known as the absolute, substantial, or convective\nderivative.\nThe thermodynamic property functions for pressure, internal\nenergy, and the chemical potentials are shown in equation (2).\nThese properties may be regarded as functions of mass density, temperature\nT, and moles\nper unit mass of the species:\np = p(m,l,n),)\nu : u(m,T,nx)\n\n(2)\n\n,x = _)_(m,l,n_)\nSubstitution of the thermodynamic\ninternal energy function into the conservation equation for internal energy converts it into an equation for the\ntemperature, and the resulting equation now contains explicit contributions\nfrom changes in composition and mass density.\nIn reacting fluids the temperature largely reflects the energy released or absorbed by chemical reactions.\nThus, we see that the thermodynamic property of internal energy determines the\ntemperature and, consequently,\nthe amount of energy released or absorbed.\nOf\ncourse, the temperature, in turn, strongly affects reaction rates and other\nphysical properties of the reacting fluid.\nThe thermodynamic\npressure equation (an equation of state) appears in the equation for the conservation of\nmomentum and thus helps to define the local flow field.\nClearly, the successful modeling of combustion processes requires two ingredients:\n(1) accurate\nthermodynamic properties for both the reactants and the products of reaction\nand (2) computational capabilities to use the properties in modeling combustion systems.\nI shall briefly describe some aspects of our work on the two topics of\nproperty estimation and property utilization.\nFirst, I shall discuss the\nestimation of free radical properties in the ideal gas state and the properties\nof nonideal solutions.\nFollowing the dlscuss_on of properties,\nI shall illustrate some of our computational\ncapabilities to use these properties to model\nthe physical systems in which reactions take place.\n\nESTIMAIION\n\nOF FREE RADICAL PROPERTIES\n\nFree radicals are highly reactive species which are usually present at low\nconcentrations\nin chemlcally reacting systems.\nThese species play an important\nrole in the chemical reaction mechanisms of combustion,\nlhey are involved in\nboth the endothermlc (heat absorbing) in_tlatlon steps of the mechanism and the\nexothermlc (heat releasing) recombination\nsteps.\nRecently Bauer and Zhang\n(ref. 3) and others have conjectured\nthat free radicals are also important\n114\n\nspecies in the mechanismof soot formation in hydrocarbon flames. Hence, the\nthermodynamic properties of free radicals are of considerable interest.\nUnfortunately, relatively\nlittle\nis knownabout the thermodynamic properties\nof radicals over the extended temperature range encountered in combustion\nsystems. Hence, we have found it necessary to estimate the thermodynamic\nproperties of the phenyl, phenoxy, and blphenyl free radicals which are likely\nto be important species in the combustion of hydrocarbons and in soot formation.\nBecause of their low concentrations and because most combustion processes\ntake place at relatively\nlow pressures and high temperatures, it is usually\nadequate to estimate free radical properties in the ideal gas state.\nThermodynamicproperties for any species in an ideal gas state can be\nestimated from the canonical partition function using the formulas of statlstlcal thermodynamics. This requires knowledge of the species\' molecular\nstructure, its vibrational and rotational energy levels, any barriers to\ninternal rotation, and the standard state heat of formation.\nThe difficulty\nis that this information is seldom available for free radicals.\nAs a result\nit becomesnecessary to estimate these data from similar, stable species whose\nmolecular properties have been measured, l hese stable, related species are\ncalled the parent species of the radicals.\nThe species benzene, phenol and\nblphenyl are suitable parent species for the radicals phenyl, phenoxy and\no-blphenyl, respectively.\nThe relation between these parent species and the\nradicals is shown in figure l. This estimation procedure is based on the work\nof Forgeteg and Berces (ref. 4). In this method it is usual to assumethat the\nbond lengths and bond angles of the radical are identical to those of the parent. Also the radical\'s vibrational frequencies are taken to be the sameas\nthose of the parent molecule except that three frequencies are eliminated for\neach atom which appears in the parent molecule but not in the radical.\nIn some\ncases, where additional information about the radical is available, the assumed\nstructure and vibrational frequencies can be adjusted to take into account this\nextra information.\nOne of the difficulties\nwith this method of estimation is the selection of\nthe appropriate frequencies for elimination.\nThe reason for this is that a\nmolecule\'s frequencies correspond to vibrations of the molecule as a whole\nrather than vibrations of a particular bond. Naturally this introduces some\nuncertainty into the resulting properties.\nTo ascertain the degree of uncertainty one might try eliminating different frequencies or, perhaps, perform the\nestimation with a different parent species.\nComparisons of the results of our estimation of phenyl and phenoxy propertles (ref. 5) with estimates madeby Benson, et al. (refs. 6 and 7) are shown\nin tables I and II for entropy and heat capacity.\nOur estimation of the phenyl\nproperties from benzene and phenol agree surprisingly well wlth each other and\nwith the room temperature values estimated by Benson. Our estimates of the\nphenoxy properties estimated from phenol also agree very well with Benson\'s\nestimates which, for this species, were available over an extended temperature\nrange.\nNONIDEAL OI_UIION ODELS\nS\nM\nThe properties of fuels and oxidizers must be accurately known if combustion processes are to be modeled properly. Since reactants, especially\nfuels, are often multlcomponent, nonldeal, liquid solutions, it is desirable\n115\n\nto have accurate mathematical\n\nrepresentations\nof the composition\ndependence\nof\ntheir\nthermodynamic\nproperties.\nSuch representations\n(solution\nmodels)\ncan be\nused to analyze,\ninterpolate\nand extrapolate\nthermodynamic\nmeasurements\nof\nnonldeal\nsolutions.\nSolution\nmodels can also be used for the calculation\nof\nequilibria.\nThe utility\nof a solution\nmodel is directly\nproportional\nto tts\nability\nto handle both a wide range of nonldeal\nbehaviors\nand a great\ndiversity\nof multlcomponent\nsystems.\nMany solutton\nmodels have been proposed,\nbut most of them have a llmtted\ncapacity\nto treat\nnonldeallty.\nSome of them either\nare only able to deal wtth\nrelatively\nsmall deviations\nfrom tdealtty\nor cannot handle the complete\ncomposition\nrange.\nOthers are limited\nto binary\nor ternary\nsolutions.\nStill\nothers\nexhibit\nundesirable\nmathematical\nbehavior.\nMany contain\nnonlinear\nparameters\nwhich greatly\ncomplicate\nthe analysis\nof experimental\nmeasurements.\nI have proposed a solution\nmodel (ref.\n8) which\novercomes most of these difficulties.\nThe model ts applicable\nto solutions\ncontaining\nan arbitrary\nnumber\nof constituents.\nIt can treat\nhighly\nnontdeal\nsolutions,\nelectrolyte\nand nonelectrolyte,\nover the complete\ncomposition\nrange.\nIt has a relatively\nsimple\nmathematical\nform, and all\nparameters\nappear linearly\nin the model.\nFinally,\nIt contains\nboth ideal\nand regular\nsolutions\nas special\ncases.\nThe model\'s\nexpression\nfor the Gtbbs free energy Is given in equation\n(3) where\nG ts the\nGtbbs free energy,\nxt\nIs the mole fraction\nof species\n1, _(t)\nrepresents\nthe capabilities\nhandles\nthree\nvery different,\nhighly\n\nof the\nnonldeal\n\nmodel by\nsolutions\n\nThe first\nsolution\nis the ethanol-heptane\nbinary\nsystem, for which there\nare three\nsets of excess enthalpy\nmeasurements\nat 30 =C.\nThe solution\nis\nendothermlc,\nand measurements\nextend\nInto\nthe very dilute\nsolution\nrange.\nThe\nrepresentation\nof the measurements\nby the to 6 show the integral heats\nof solution while the excess heat capacity is shown in figure 7. Once again\nthe model gives more than an adequate representation of the experimental\nmeasurements.\nCHEMICAL\nREACTIONQUII.IBRIA\nE\nSometimesthe behavior of chemically reacting systems can be predicted\nquite well on the basis of chemical equilibrium.\nOver the years we have\ndeveloped a capability to perform computations reliably for several applications involving chemical reactions (refs. lO to 16).\nThe calculations are\nbased on free energy minimization and assume that the gaseous phase behaves\nideally, that the condensed phases are pure liquids and solids, and that the\nvolume of the condensed phases is negligible.\nThe computations can evaluate\ncomposition and, also, thermodynamic and transport properties for complex\nchemical systems involving up to 20 chemical elements and 600 reaction\nproducts.\nComposition and properties can be calculated for a thermodynamic\nstate\ndefined by pressure and one of the three variables:\ntemperature, enthalpy, or\nentropy.\nAlternatively,\nthe thermodynamic state can be specified by assigning\nthe volume and one of the three variables temperature, internal energy, or\nentropy.\nIn addition to the calculation of compositions and properties for an\nassigned thermodynamic\nstate, we can also compute the results for three dlfferent one-dlmenslonal\nflow processes neglecting heat transfer effects:\n(1) rocket nozzle expansions, (2) incident and reflected shocks, and\n(3) Chapman-3ouguet\ndetonations.\nThe rocket nozzle and shock calculations can\nbe performed for either equilibrium or frozen compositions.\nTable III is an\nexample of the calculation of the composition and of the thermodynamic\nand\ntransport properties for a simple system composed of hydrogen and oxygen at\nassigned pressures and temperatures.\nTable IV illustrates the results for the\ncalculation of a one-dlmenslonal\nshock In a hydrogen-oxygen\nmixture diluted\nwith argon.\n\nMODELING\n\nTHE SPARK-IGNITED\n\nINTERNAL\n\nCOMBUSTION\n\nENGINE\n\nThe spark-ignlted\ninternal combustion engine is an example of a chemically\nreacting system for which purely thermodynamic predictions are inadequate.\nA\nrealistic model must take into account not only thermodynamic properties but\nalso heat transfer rates, finite rate chemistry (chemical kinetics), and\nintake and exhaust flow rates.\nThese effects lead to a complex model and dlfficult calculations.\nA schematic representation of the operation of the spark-lgnlted\ninternal\ncombustion engine is shown in figure 8. Each cycle of the operation can be\ndescribed qualitatively\nby dividing the cycle into four parts, each part\nspanning\n_\nradlans (180 \xc2\xb0) of crankangle.\nEach part corresponds approximately\nto one of four processes taking place in the engine.\nThe parts are given names\nwhich are reasonably descriptive of the four processes:\n(1) the intake stroke,\n(2) the compression stroke, (3) the power stroke and (4) the exhaust stroke.\nA fuel-alr mixture enters the cylinder during the intake stroke and is compressed by the piston during the compression stroke.\nThe compressed working\nfluid Is ignited and expanded during the power stroke and is expelled from the\nI17\n\ncylinder\nduring the exhaust\nstroke.\nBecause matter\nenters\nand leaves\nthe\ncylinder,\nthe Internal\ncombustion\nengine\nIs an open system.\nFurthermore,\nthe\nburned gases are not completely\nexpelled\nfrom the cylinder,\nand thus the\nresidual\ngases provide\na "memory"\nof each cycle for the subsequent\ncycle.\nEach\ncycle occurs rapidly\nwith\na cycle\nrepetition\nrate which\nranges from about 25\nto 250 msec.\nThts cycle repetition\nrate Is comparable\nto the time scale for\nthe complex chemistry\nwhlch\ntakes place during\ncombustion,\nand thus\nthe rates\nof chemlcal\nreactions\nbecome Important.\nThe englne has a movable\nboundary\n(the\npiston)\nand a complex geometry.\nFtnally,\nthe operation\nof the engine Is\nnonrepeatable,\nand the engine experlences\n"cycle-to-cycle"\nvartatlons.\nThese\nfeatures\nof the Internal\ncombustion\nengine make Its modeltng\ndifficult\nIndeed.\nAdditionally,\noften there\nts Insufficient\nInformation\nto descrtbe\ncompletely\nthe details\nof the engine operation\neven If It were mathematlcally\nand numerIcally\npossible.\nI have chosen to develop\n(ref.\n17) a hierarchy\nof models of differing\ncomplexity\nIn order to accommodate the varying\namounts of Information\navailable\nIn particular\ncases.\nThe models\nrange from a simple model requiring\nonly\nthermodynamic\nproperties\n(modeling\nlevel\n1) to a complex model demanding full\ncombustion\nkinetics,\ntransport\nproperties\nand poppet valve flow characteristics\n(modeling\nlevel\n5).\nThe members of the hierarchy\nare classified\naccording\nto\ntheir\ntreatment\nof several\nImportant\nfeatures\nof the Internal\ncombustion\nengine\nas shown In table\nV.\nThe description\nof the operation\nof the Internal\ncombustlon\nenglne as betng divided\nInto\nfour parts\nIs adequate\nfor a qualltatlve\ndescription.\nIt Is, however,\nInadequate\nto descrlbe\nthe operation\nof a real\nengine correspond%ng\nto modeling\nat levels\n4 and 5.\nThe timing\nfor the Initiation\nand cessation\nof Important\nevents\nIn a more realistic\ncycle Is sketched\nIn ftgure\n9.\nThis figure,\noften\nreferred\nto as an Indicator\ndlagram,\nIs a plot\nof working\nflutd\npressure\nas a function\nof cylinder\nvolume for one complete\ncycle\nspanning 4e radlans\n(720 \xc2\xb0 ) of crankangle.\nIt should be noted that\nbetween stations\n7 and 2 the tntake\nand the exhaust\nvalves are simultaneously\nopen; this\ncorresponds\nto what Is called\nthe valve-overlap\nportion\nof the\ncycle.\nI shall\nuse the fuel-rich\ncombustlon\nof gaseous propane lTZ18\n\nFigure 15.\n\n133\n\nN87-20275\nEFFECT\n\nOF FLAME-IUBE\n\nHEAD STRUCIURE\n\nON COMBUSIION\n\nCHAMBER\n\nPERFORMANCE\n\nGu Minqql\nShenyang Aero-Englne Research Institute\nShenyang, People\'s Republic of China\n\nThis paper presents the experimental combustion performance of a premixed, pilot-type flame tube wlth various head structures.\nThe test study\ncovers an extensive area:\nefficiency of the combustion chamber, quality of\nthe outlet temperature field, limit of the fuel-lean blowout, ignition performance at ground starting, and carbon deposition.\nAs a result of these\ntests, a nozzle was found which fits the premlxed pilot flame tube well.\nThe\nuse of this nozzle optimized the performance of the combustion chamber.\nlhe tested models had premlxed pilot chambers with two types of alr-filmcooling structures, six types of venturl-tube structures, and secondary fuel\nnozzles with two small spray-cone angles.\n\nINTRODUCIION\nAfter a period of engine operation, a premlxed pilot-type flame tube\n(fig. l) with a pressurized\nfuel nozzle may have difficulty in starting.\nSometimes the pilot chamber may not ignite at all; this seriously affects the\nstarting performance of the engine.\nThis problem is generally believed to be\ndue to carbon deposition in the premlxed pilot chamber (8 g carbon deposited\nin a single premlxed pilot chamber after 190 min of running).\nIn the case of\nhigh-load running, the gas temperature in the center of the premixed pilot\nchamber can be raised as high as 1200 \xc2\xb0C; this can make the high-denslty fuel\nspray crack and deposit on the downstream section of the nozzle.\nIn addition,\nthe wall temperature would be high, causing the fuel spray to form coke on the\nwall, which (coupled with fuel deposition) would make the wall rough.\nThen the\ncarbon particles would hit the cores of deposition and would be deposited on\nthe wall.\nThus, the wall becomes uneven and very rough.\nWhen the fuel spray\nagain hits the wall and flows on its uneven soft carbon layer, the fuel film\nis also very uneven (unlike the film on a smooth metal wall).\nAs a result, the\nspray quality becomes very poor and the engine fails to start.\nThe objective of this experiment was to improve the ground starting performance while not damaging the other types of combustion performance.\nThe\nstudy concentrated on the effect of premlxed pilot chamber structures and\nnozzle spray angles on combustion performance.\nThis paper presents a study of the effect on combustion of (1) prem_xed pilot chambers with air-film-coollng\nand venturl-tube structures and\n(2) small-angle secondary nozzles.\nIt emphasizes their effects on ground\nstarting performance and on carbon deposition within the pilot chambers.\n\n_ESI MODELS\nPremlxed\n\nPilot Chamber\n\nWith Air-Film-Cooling\n\nStructure\n\nIt is known that the alr-film-cooling\nstructure enables free carbon\nparticles to be carried away by the film-coollng air flow, thus reducing\n135\n\n.....\n\np\n\nNOT\n\nor\n\navoiding the contact of these particles with the wall.\nThis helps reduce the\nwail temperature.\nTwo models were evaluated (fig: 2).\nEighty film-coollng\nholes (@2) were added to the wall of these two premixed pilot chambers.\nThe\nratio of the cooling-hole opening area to the total opening area in the flame\ntube wall was 0.022.\nThe air velocity in the outlet of the alr-film-coollng\nring was 15 to 26 m/sec.\n\nPremixed\n\nPilot Chamber\n\nWith\n\nVenturi-Tube\n\nStructure\n\nA venturi tube was mounted in the rear of the swirler (fig. 3).\nIt made\nthe fuel injected from the nozzle hit the venturl-tube wall and then mix wlth\nthe air coming from the inner section of the swirler, thus avoiding contact of\nthe fuel wlth the flame tube wall.\nIn addition, because the venturi tube was\nused, the position of the reclrculation\nzone moved further downstream of the\nnozzle; as a result, the temperature\nof the carbon-generatlng\nzone downstream\nof the fuel nozzle was reduced.\nThis helped reduce the carbon particles.\n\nfig.\nwhen\n\nS_x venturi tubes of different sizes were evaluated.\n(See table I and\n4.) The ratio of the outer to the inner area of the swlrler was 1.16\nthe venturi tube was mounted.\n\nSecondary\n\nNozzle With\n\nSmall\n\nSpray\n\nAngle\n\nFuel entered from the centrifugal nozzle in the ignition process in order\nto reduce or eliminate the probability of the sprayed fuel hitting the" wall.\n1wo kinds of small spray-angle secondary nozzles were studied.\n(See table II\nand fig. 5.) The spray angle was made small enough that the spray would not\nhit the inner wall of the premlxed pilot chamber.\nThus, the fuel spray cone\nwas not impaired.\nThe fuel-alr ratio was appropriate near the ignitor, and a\ngood starting performance was maintained (even if there was carbon deposition\nin the pilot chamber).\n\nRESUI_IS AND ANALYSIS\nAmount of Carbon\n\nDeposition\n\nin Premlxed\n\nPilot\n\nChamber\n\nlhe experimental results for various models are listed in table III.\n(Results for the original flame tube are included for comparison.)\nThe\nresults indicate that the venturi tube is the best structure for eliminating\ncarbon deposition.\nThere was almost no carbon deposition in the pilot chamber,\nand there was only a small amount of carbon deposition in the 20- to 30-mm\nposition downstream of the pilot chamber exit.\nThe objective of reducing carbon deposition in the pilot chamber was achieved.\nResults from the water analog test indicate that there was no reclrculation zone in the venturl tube.\nThe recirculation\nzone in the premlxed pilot\nchamber was located far downstream of the nozzle (fig. 6). The heat radiation\nfrom the flame-tube center to the nozzle was blocked outside of the venturi\ntube, thus reducing the temperature downstream of the nozzle.\nThis had a\ndeterminate\neffect on the reduction of the carbon particles.\nIt was for\n\n136\n\nthis reason that the amount of carbon deposition in the flame tube with a venfurl tube was obviously reduced.\nAdding an alr-film-coollng\nstructure in the premlxed pilot chamber helped\nreduce carbon deposition.\nThe reduction rate was usually as much as 20 to 30\npercent near the alr-film\nexits.\nBut, at the exits of the air films, the air\nvelocity was small, so was the alr-flow momentum. Nevertheless, the momentum\nof the fuel injected from the nozzle was rather large, so the film flow could\nnot prevent the fuel droplets from hitting the wall.\nIt is only possible for\nthe film air to bring away free carbon particles.\nFor this reason, this\nstructure is not very effective in reducing carbon deposition.\nGround Starting Performance\nThe results for five models at the ground starting ignition condition are\nshown in figures 7 and 8 where V2 is the inlet velocity of the combustion\nchamber. The curves of figure 7 indicate that there is an optimal value for\neach curve.\nThis velocity ranges from 15 to 20 m/sec.\nance was best for the flame tube with a model I venturl\n\nThe ignition\ntube.\n\nperform-\n\nFigure 8 indicates that the ground starting ignition performance\nimproved\ngreatly when model I and II nozzles were used under carbon deposition conditions in the premlxed pilot chamber.\nAt 17.5 m/sec of inlet velocity the\nfuel-lean ignition limit improved; the limiting alr-fuel ratio increased from\n36.8 to 63.2.\nIt is readily seen from these results that, under the carbon\ndeposition condition in the premlxed pilot chamber, adoption of a small-angle\nsecondary nozzle obviously improves the ground starting ignition performance.\nIn addition, it is known from the curves that when carbon is deposited in\nthe premlxed pilot chamber the alr-fuel ratio of the fuel-lean ignition limit\ndecreases from 47 to 36. This confirms that carbon deposition in the prem_xed\npilot chamber deteriorates the ground starting ignition performance.\n\nOutlet\nTable\n\nTemperature\n\nIV shows the test results\n\nField of Combustion\nof various\n\nChamber\n\nventurl-tube\n\nmodels.\n\nIt is\n\nreadily seen from the table that the size of the venturl tube mounted in the\npremlxed pilot chamber has a great effect on the combustor outlet temperature\nfield.\nThe outlet temperature distribution factor (OIDF, table IV) of the\nmodel V combustion chamber was 0.155; this was much better than that for the\noriginal model (0.229).\nlhe OIDF\'s of models I and II were similar to that of\nthe original model, but those of models IV and VI were inferior to that of the\noriginal model.\nlhe convergent angle of the front section and the outlet angle of the\nventurl tube have an obvious effect on the combustor outlet temperature distribution.\nWhen the convergent angle of the front section of the venturl tube\nincreases, the uniformity of the combustor outlet temperature field decreases.\nWhen the convergent angle was increased from 27o34 \' to 67o22 \', the OTDF\nincreased from 0.209 to 0.301.\nWhen the venturl-tube\noutlet angle became\nrather small, excessive fuel concentrated in the center of the flame tube;\nthis caused the temperature to rise in the center region and the outlet\n\n137\n\ntemperature\ndistribution\nof the venturt\ntube from\n\nto deteriorate.\nFor\n88 \xc2\xb0 to 56 \xc2\xb0 increased\n\nexample,\nreducing\nthe outlet\nthe OTDF from 0.155 to 0.301.\n\nangle\n\nAdoption\nof a secondary\nnozzle with\na small spray angle also improved\nthe\nuniformity\nof the combustor\noutlet\ntemperature\nfteldthe OTDF of model II was\n0.175,\nthe radial\ntemperature\ndistribution\nfactor\n(RTDF, table\nIV) was 0.077.\n\nStability\n\nof\n\nCombustion\n\nChamber\n\nFigure\n9 shows experlmental\nresults\nof the fuel-lean\nblowout\nllmlt\nfor\nvarious\nmodels.\nThe shapes of the curve\nare nearly\nthe same for various\nmodels.\nThe fuel-lean\nblowout\nllmtt\nfor the model II venturl\ntube was wider\nbecause this\nventurt\ntube had no throat\nsection;\nthus,\na small rectrculatlon\nzone was formed inside\nthe venturi\ntube.\nThe size of the rectrculatlon\nzone\nwas limited\n\nto\n\nthe\n\nnozzle\n\ndiameter\n\nrange\n\n(fig.\n\n10).\n\nThe fuel-lean blowout limits of the model III, IV, and V venturl tubes were\nnarrower.\nIt Is known from water analog test results that there is a highspeed rotating flow in the throat section of a venturl tube (fig. ll).\nThls\nunstable flow deteriorates the fuel-lean blowout limit.\nWhen a secondary nozzle with a small spray angle was adopted,\nlean blowout limit improved In the wlde velocity range.\n\nthe fuel-\n\nCONCLUSIONS\nThis\n\nstudy led to the following\n\nconclusions:\n\n(I) Adoption of the secondary nozzle wlth a small spray angle not only\nsolves the engine\'s starting ignition problem when there Is carbon deposition\nIn the premlxed pilot chamber but also extends the ground starting ignition\nrange and improves the uniformity of the combustor outlet temperature\nfield.\n(2) Adoption of an alr-fllm-coollng\nstructure reduces the amount of carbon\ndeposition on the wall of the premlxed pilot chamber.\nBut since there Is only\nlimited cooling alr available, it Is not easy to totally eliminate the carbon\ndeposition In the premlxed pilot chamber.\n(3) Mounting the venturl tube in the rear of the swlrler Is effective In\neliminating carbon deposition In the premlxed pilot chamber.\nBut the geometry\nof the venturl tube affects the uniformity of the _ombustor outlet temperature\nfield and the ground starting ignition performance; therefore, characteristic\ngeometric parameters of the venturl tube have to be elaborately adjusted.\n\nBIBLIOGRAPHY\nGu, M.Q.:\nStudy Holdeman\nLewis Research Center\nCleveland, Ohlo\n\nThis paper presents experimental and analytical results of the mixing of\nsingle, double, and opposed rows of Jets wlth an isothermal or variabletemperature maln stream In a straight duct.\nThls study was performed to investigate flow and geometric variations typical of the complex, three-dlmenslonal\nflow field In the dilution zone of gas-turblne-englne\ncombustion chambers.\nThe principal results, shown experimentally\nand analytically, were the\nfollowing:\n(1) variations in orifice slze and spacing can have a significant\neffect on the temperature profiles; (2) similar distributions\ncan be obtained,\nindependent of orifice dlameter, if momentum-flux\nratio and orifice spacing are\ncoupled; (3) a flrst-order approximation of the mixing of jets wlth a variabletemperature maln stream can be obtained by superimposing the maln-stream and\njets-ln-an-lsothermal-crossflow\nprofiles; (4) the penetration of Jets issuing\nfrom slanted slots Is similar to that of Jets from circular holes, but the\nmixing is slower and is asymmetric wlth respect to the Jet centerplanes, which\nshift laterally wlth increasing downstream distance, (5) double rows of Jets\nglve temperature distributions\nsimilar to those from a single row of equally\nspaced, equal-area circular holes; (6) for opposed rows of Jets, wlth the orifice centerllnes In llne, the optimum ratio of orifice spacing to duct height\nIs one-half the optimum value for slngle-slde injection at the same momentumflux ratio; and (7) for opposed rows of Jets, with the orifice centerllnes\nstaggered, the optimum ratio of orifice spacing to duct height Is twice the\noptimum value for slngle-slde injection at the same momentum-flux\nratio.\nIn illustrating these results, the mean temperature measurements are compared wlth profiles calculated using an empirical model based on assumed vertical profile similarity and superposltlon and wlth distributions\ncalculated\nusing a three-dlmenslonal\nelllptlc code that had a standard k-_ turbulence\nmodel.\nThe empirical model predictions are very good within the range of the\ngenerating experiments, and the numerical model results, although they exhibit\ntoo little mixing, correctly describe the effects of the principal flow and\ngeometric variables.\n\nSYMBOLS\nAj/A m\n\nJet to maln-stream\nfor one-slde\n\narea\n\ninjection\n\nratio, A_/A\nm-\n\nand (_/2)/[(S/Ho)(Ho/D)\n\ninjection\nC\n\n(S/Ho)_-\',\n\neq. (1)\n\ndifference\n\nratio\n\nat\n\nYc\n\nratio\n\nabove\n\n(\xc3\xb7) or below\n\n(-) the\n\nINTRODUCIION\nThe problem of jets in crossflow has been rather extensively treated in\nthe literature, to the point that it can almost be called a classical threedimensional\nflow problem.\nAlthough these studies have all contributed\nto the\nunderstanding\nof the general problem, the information obtained in any given\n150\n\nORIGINAL\n\nPAGE\n\nIS\n\nOF POOR\n\nQUALITY\n\nstudy is naturally determined by the motivating application\nnot satisfy the specific needs of diverse applications.\n\nand therefore\n\nmay\n\nConsideration\nof dilution zone mixing in gas-turbine combustion chambers\nhas motivated several previous studies of the mixing characteristics\nof Jets\ninjected normally into a ducted crossflow (refs. l to 12).\nOne factor making\nthe combustor dilution zone jet-ln-crossflow\napplication unique is that it is\na confined mixing problem - lO to 50 percent of the total flow enters through\nthe dilution jets.\nThe result is that the equilibrium temperature of the exiting flow may differ significantly\nfrom that of the entering maln-stream\nflow.\nlo control or tailor the combustor exit temperature pattern, we must be able\nto characterize\nthe exit distribution in terms of the upstream flow and geometric variables,\nlhls requires that the entire flow field be either known or\nmodeled.\nEmpirical correlation of experimental data can provide an excellent predictive capability within the parameter range of the generating experiments\n(e.g., refs. 4 to 6), but empirical models must be used with caution, or not\nat all, outside this range.\nPhysical modeling, in various levels of sophistication and complexity, may be used to obviate this weakness.\nIn this regard,\nseveral one- and two-dimenslonal,\nintegral and differential Jet-ln-crossflow\nmodels have been developed and shown to give, for example, trajectory predictions that are in good agreement with experiments,\nlhese models may provide\ninsight into the dominant physical mechanism(s) and may predict some of the\ncharacteristic\nparameters well, but they rarely provide sufficient information\nto quantify the flow field in three coordinate directions.\nRecently, rapid advances have been made in the capability of computational\nfluid dynamics models and in their application to complex flows such as jets\nin crossflow (refs. 13 to 16).\nThese models are, however, still in the development and verification stage.\nThey have been shown to be capable of predicting trends in complex flows, but their capability to provide accurate,\nquantltive,\nand grld-lndependent\ncalculations of these flows has not yet been\ndemonstrated\n(refs. 14 to 16).\n\nPREVIOUS\n\nRESULTS\n\nAND THE CURRENT\n\nSTUDY\n\nlhe study in references 17 to 19 was performed to extend the available\nexperimental\ndata on, and the empirical correlations of, the thermal mixing of\nmultiple jets in crossflows so that they would include geometric and flow\nvariations characteristic\nof gas turbine combustion chambers - namely, variable\ntemperature main stream, flow area convergence, nonclrcular orifices, and\ndouble and opposed rows of jets.\nThese experiments are a direct extension of\nthose in reference I. The effect on the dimensionless\ntemperature distributions of varying the jet to maln-stream density ratio, the momentum-flux\nratio,\nand the orifice size and spacing are presented in reference 2.\nFrom the data in reference I, an empirical model was developed (refs. 4\nand 5) for predicting the temperature field downstream of a row of jets mixing\nwith a confined crossflow.\nThe effects of separately varying the independent\nflow and geometric variables and the relations among these variables which\noptimize the mixing are reviewed in reference 12.\nlhis study was conducted\nusing an interactive microcomputer\nprogram that is based on the empirical model\nof reference 5 ....\n..,\n151\n\nThe results of these investigations may be summarizedas follows:\n(I)\nm_xlng improves with increasing downstream distance; (2) the momentum-flux\nratio is the most significant flow variable; (3) the effect of density ratio\nis small at constant momentum flux ratio; (4) at any given momentum-flux\nratio, decreasing orifice spacing while maintaining\na constant orifice diameter\nreduces penetration and increases lateral uniformity;\n(5) increasing orifice\ndiameter while maintaining a constant spaclng-to-dlameter\nratio improves penetration but increases lateral nonunlformlty;\n(6) increasing orifice diameter\nat a constant orifice spacing increases the magnitude of the temperature difference, but jet penetration and profile shape remain similar; (7) profiles for\nconditions with equivalent coupling of orifice spacing and momentumflux\nratio\nshow similar distributions; and (8) smaller momentum-flux\nratios (larger spacing) require a greater downstream distance for equivalent mixing.\nResults from the experiments\nin references 17 to 19 that are considered\nin this paper include the effects of variations in orifice size and spacing,\ncoupled spacing and momentum-flux\nratio, variable temperature main stream,\nnoncircular orifices, and double and opposed rows of Inllne and staggered jets.\nAlso, temperature field measurements\nfrom several experiments are compared with\ndistributions\ncalculated using an empirical model based on assumed vertical\nprofile similarity and superposltlon\n(refs. 17 to 19) and using a threedimensional\nelliptic code with a standard k-_ turbulence model (ref. 14).\nThe\nresults show the capability of these models to predict the effects of the\nprincipal flow and geometric variables.\nA more complete presentation of the experimental\nresults and a discussion\nof the empirical modeling performed in this study are given in references l,\n4, and ll to 19. Selected experimental and analytical results from these\nstudies and from reference 14 are also given in references 2, 5, and 20 to 23.\n\nFLOW FIFI_D DESCRIPIION\nFigure l shows a schematic of the dilution Jet flow field for jet injection from the top wall.\nThe temperature field results are presented in threedimensional oblique views of the temperature difference ratio\ne:\nT\n\nm\n\ne-1\n\n- l\n\n-T\nm\n\n(1)\nj\n\nA sequence of experimental profiles of this parameter\ntions downstream of the injection plane is shown in figure\ndimensional plots the temperature distribution\nis shown in\nnormal to the main flow direction\nx. Note that\n\nat several Ioca2.\nIn the threethe\ny,z-planes\nand\nz are, respecthe Jet fluid is\n\nidentified by the larger values of the\ne parameter (i.e., e = 1 if T = lj,\nand\ne = 0 if l = Tm).\nlhe equilibrium\ne\nfor any configuration\nis equal\nto the fraction of the total flow entering through the dilution jets wj/w l.\nlhe orifice configurations\ninvestigated are shown in figure 3. The primary independent geometric variables for each orifice configuration\nare the\nspacing between adjacent orifices\nS, the orifice diameter\nD (for nonclrcular\norifices, th_s is taken as the diameter of a circle of equal area), and, for\ndouble rows, the axial spacing between rows\nSx.\nlhese are expressed in\n152\n\ndimensionless form as the ratio of the orifice spacing to duct height S/Ho,\nthe ratio of the duct height to orifice diameter Ho/D, and the ratio of the\naxial spacing to the duct height Sx/HO.\nEXPERIMENIAL\nCONSIDERAIIONS\nlhe dilution-jet\nmixing characteristics were determined by measuring temperature and pressure distributions\nwith a vertical-rake\nprobe positioned at\ndifferent axial and lateral stations.\nThis probe had 20 thermocouple elements.\nA 20-element total-pressure rake and a 20-element statlc-pressure rake were\nlocated nominally 5 mm(0.05 HO) on each side of the thermocouple rake. The\ncenter-to-center\nspacing between sensors on each rake was 0.05 HO.\nlhis probe traversed a matrix of 48 to 64 z,x-plane survey locations.\nlhe flow field was mappedin the z-direction over a distance of l or 1.5 times\nthe hole spacing S at intervals of S/lO. For most tests, the x,y-plane\ncontaining the orifice centerllne (centerplane) was at the center of the span\nsurveyed; that is, data surveys were from midplane to midplane.\nMeasurementsin the x-dlrectlon were madeat up to five planes with\n0.25 < X/H0 < 2. Because the objective in this application is to identify\ndilution-zone configurations that will provide a desired mixing pattern within\na given combustor length, the downstreamstations are defined in intervals of\nthe duct height H rather than intervals of the orifice diameter D.\n0\nFLOW\nFIELDMODELS\nEmpirical\nlhe empirical model for the temperature field downstream of Jets mixing\nwith a confined crossflow is based on the observation that properly nondlmenslonallzed vertical temperature profiles can be expressed in the following\nself-similar\nform (ref. 5) for any location in the flow field:\n\nE yyc21\n\ne - em_n\n+ - e xp - In 2\nec - emTn\nwhere\n\ne\n-\n\nis the temperature\n_\n\ndifference\n\nratio\n\n(2)\n\nW\xc2\xb1\nI/2\n\nat vertical\n\nlocation\n\ny, and\n\n-\n\neml n, emi n, Wl/2, Wl/2, ec, and\n\nYc\n\nare scaling\n\nparameters\n\nas shown\n\nin\n\nfigure 4. Correlations\nhave been developed for each of these in terms of the\nindependent variables\nJ, S/D, Ho/D, Z/S, and\nx/H O. The correlations\nin\nreference 5 for a single row of jets in a uniform temperature crossflow have\nbeen extended for predicting the temperature field downstream of single, double, or opposed rows of Jets, either Inllne or staggered, injected into an\nisothermal or nonJsothermal\nmain stream, with or without flow-area convergence\n(refs. 17 to Ig).\n\n153\n\nNumerical\nlhe numerical code used in this investigation\nis based on the USARTL\nthree-dlmenslonal,\nfully elliptic, turbulent flow model (ref. 24) and uses\npressure and velocity as the main hydrodynamic\nvariables.\nThis code, or slm%far versions thereof, has been used in previous validation and assessment\nstudies (refs. 14 to 16).\nThe governing equations are represented by flnlte-dlfference\napproximations on a staggered grid system.\nThe differencing\ntechnique employed is\nhybrid for convective terms with central differencing\nof all other terms.\nThe\nveloclty-pressure\ncoupling is handled by the SIMPLE algorithm of Patankar and\nSpalding (refs. 25 and 26).\nUniform velocities and mass flow rates were used\nat all inflow boundaries.\nThe code contained a conventional\nk-c turbulence\nmodel, and standard values of the constants\nCD, Cl, and\nC2 were used\n(i.e., CD = o.og, C1 = 1.44, C2 = l.g2).\nThe rms turbulence intensity was\nchosen to be 4.5 percent of the local mean velocity, the inlet length scale was\n2 percent of the Jet diameter and duct height for the Jet and main stream,\nrespectively,\nand the turbulent Prandtl number was 0.9 for all calculations.\n\nRESULTS\n\nAND DISCUSSION\n\nThe in figure 8(b).\nAs noted in reference 23, the penetration\nand mixing of jets from\nthe slanted slots are noticeably less than those of Jets from the circular\nholes.\nThe normally symmetric vortex pair is asymmetric in this case, as is\napparent in the experimental profiles in figure II (and in the figures in\nref. 23). These profiles also show that the centerplanes of the jets shift\nlaterally with increasing downstream distance.\nThe empirical model calculations\ninclude a modification\nto account for the\nobserved centerplane shift, but they do not model the asymmetry (refs. 19\nand 23).\nThe numerical calculations for this case exhibit both the centerplane\nshift and the asymmetry, and they are good in the context of the qualitative\nagreement seen throughout the comparisons given in this paper.\n\nDouble\n\nRows of Holes\n\nFigure 12 shows experimental and calculated temperature distributions\nfor\nan orifice plate with two Inline rows of jets (Sx/H 0 = 0.5) from circular\norifices.\nIt was observed from the experimental profiles in reference 23 that\nthe two configurations have very similar temperature distributions,\nand this\nis seen in the calculated profiles as well.\nIn this case the empirical model\ncalculations are derived by superimposing\nthe distributions\nfrom the two rows.\nBoth experimental and calculated temperature distributions\nare shown in\nfigure 13 for a double-row configuration\nwhen\nSx/H 0 = 0.25 and the trailing\nrow has twice as many orifices as the lead row.\nNote that the orifice area is\n.....................\n_^" in\nUUIII\nI\nu vw\nthe same for\nDoLr!\nFOW_\xe2\x80\xa2\nurue_e pruTules\nshow the _--_,,a, I_\nu,_ _^\n_,,= I=_\n^_\nestablishing\nthe jet penetration\nand flrst-order profile shape (ref. 23). in\n\nLIMIIATIONS\n\ngive\n\nAND APPLICABILITY\nEmpl rl cal\n\nlhe empirical model results presented here show that correlating experimental data can provide an excellent predictive capability within the parameter\nrange of the generating experiments, provided the experimental\nresults are\nconsistent with the assumptions made in the empirical model.\nThese models\nmust, however, be used with caution, or not at all, outside this range.\nlhe ranges of the experimental variables on which this empirical model was\nbased are given in table III. The density ratio, momentum-flux\nratio, orifice\nspacing, and orifice size were the primary independent variables.\nThis table\nalso gives ratios that are derived from the primary variables:\nthe orifice to\nmain-stream\narea ratio, the jet-to-total mass flow split, and the parameter\ncoupling the spacing and momentum-flux\nratio.\nNot all combinations of the\nprimary variables in the table were tested; only those combinations which are\nwithin the range given for the derived variables represent conditions that are\nwithin the range of the experiments.\nExamining the results in figures 5 to 17 in the context of equation (3)\nsuggests that, in general, the empirical model provides good temperature field\npredictions for slngle-slde injection when l < C < 5. Similarly, good predictions are obtained for opposed Inllne Jets provided that 0.5 < C < 2.5. This\nmodel does not work well for impinging flows because the experimental\ntemperature distributions\nare not consistent with the assumption of Gausslan profile\nsimilarity in the empirical model.\nThe experimental profiles for conditions\ngiving optimum mixing in opposed staggered-Jet\nconfigurations\nare also somewhat\nat variance with the model assumptions;\nin these cases, satisfactory agreement\n\nA major weakness of the empirical model used here in (refs. 17 to 19) and\nin previous versions (refs. 4 and 5) is that the form of the correlations precludes their use for seml-conflned\nflows (large\nHo/D\nor S/D), slngle-jet\nflows, or flows in which it is known a priori that the primary assumptions\nin\nthe model will be invalid.\n\n158\n\nNumerical\nlhe numerical model is not subject to the inherent limitation\nof the\nempirical model regarding profile shape and confinement. Thus, threedimensional codes can provide calculations for complex flows for which the\nassumptions in the empirical model are known to be invalid or outside the range\nof available experiments. Furthermore, numerical models provide calculations\nfor all flow field parameters of interest, not just those that happen to have\nbeen empirically correlated.\nlhe numerical calculations correctly show the trends which result from\nvariation of the independent flow and geometric variables, although the results\nconsistently exhibit too little\nmixing. The numerical model calculations for\nthe slanted slots and staggered Jet cases are encouraging because the experimental data for these cases show profiles that are not consistent with the\nprimary assumptions in the empirical model.\nlhe numerical calculations performed are shown to be grid sensitive, and\nfalse diffusion is known to be present. Uncertainties also exist in these\ncalculations regarding the validity of turbulence model assumptions and due to\nunmeasured(and hence assumed) boundary conditions.\nThe results shown here are\nnot intended to represent the best agreement possible from numerical models at\nthis time. Better temperature field agreement could undoubtedly have been\nachieved by adjusting model constants and/or inlet boundary conditions.\nBut,\nsince this was not necessary to satisfy the present objective of evaluating the\npotential of these codes vis-a-vis combustor dilution zone flow fields, and\nbecause the meantemperature was the only parameter compared, no adjustments\nwere made.\nlhus, as with previous assessmentsin references 14 to 16, threedimensional calculations, such as those in this paper, should be considered as\nonly qualitatively\naccurate at this time, and three-dlmenslonal codes of this\ntype are useful primarily in guiding design changes or in perturbation analyses. lhe three-dlmenslonal code used herein, although sufficiently\npromising\nto justify\nfurther development and assessment, is not a practical tool for\ngeneral engineering use in its present form. Codes with improved numerics,\naccuracy, and turbulence models should provide more quantitative predictions.\nCONCLUSIONS\nlhe principal\nare as follows:\n\nconclusions from the experimental results\n\nI. Variations\na significant\n\nwhen\n\nof momentum-flux\nratio and of orifice\neffect on the flow distribution.\n\n2. Similar distributions\ncan be obtained, independent\nmomentum-flux\nratio and orifice spacing are coupled.\n\nreviewed herein\n\nsize and spacing\n\nof orifice\n\ndiameter,\n\n3. A flrst-order approximation\nof the mixing of jets with a variabletemperature main stream can be achieved by superimposing\nthe Jets-in-anIsothermal-maln-stream\nand upstream profiles.\n\n159\n\nhave\n\n4. lhe penetration and mixing of Jets issuing from 45\xc2\xb0 slanted slots are\nless than those from equlvalent-area circular holes.\n5. With the sameorifice spacing in (at least) the lead row, double rows\nof Jets have temperature distributions\nsimilar to those from a single row of\nequally spaced, equlvalent-area circular orifices.\n6. For opposed rows of jets, with the orifice centerllnes in llne, the\noptimum ratio of orifice spacing to duct height is one-half the optimum value\nfor slngle-slde injection at the samemomentum-flux ratio.\n7. For opposed rows of Jets with the orifice centerllnes staggered, the\noptimum ratio of orifice spacing to duct height is twice the optimum value for\nslngle-slde injection at the samemomentum-flux ratio.\nTemperature field measurementsfrom the experiments cited previously are\ncomparedwith distributions\ncalculated using an empirical model based on\nassumedvertical profile similarity\nand superposltlon and with calculations\nmadeusing a three-dlmenslonal elliptic\ncode with a standard k-c turbulence\nmodel. The results can be summarizedas follows:\nEmpirical model calculations provide very good results for modeled parameters within the range of experiments whenever the primary assumptions in the\nmodel are satisfied.\nThree-dlmenslonal code calculations madein thls study correctly approximate the trends which result from varying the independent flow and geometric\nvariables, but they consistently exhibit too little\nmixing. (The advantage of\nthese models is that they can predict all flow field quantities,\nflows outside\nthe range of experiments, or flows where empirical assumptions are invalid.)\nNumerical calculations should yield more quantitative predictions with\nimprovements in numerics, accuracy, and turbulence models.\nREFERENCES\nI. Blttker\nNASA Lewis Research Center\nCleveland, Ohio\n\nThis paper describes NASA Lewis\' studies of complex reacting systems at\nhigh temperature.\nThe changes which occur are the result of many different\nchemical reactions occurring at the same time.\nBoth an experimental and a\ntheoretical\napproach are needed to fully understand what happens in these systems.\nThe latter approach is discussed herein.\nWe present the differential\nequations which describe the chemical and thermodynamic\nchanges, and we\ndescribe their solution by numerical techniques using a detailed chemical\nmechanism.\nSeveral different comparisons of computed results with experimental measurements\nare also given.\nThese include the computation of (1) species\nconcentration\nprofiles in batch and flow reactions, (2) rocket performance\nin\nnozzle expansions, and (3) pressure versus time profiles in hydrocarbon\nignition processes.\nThe examples illustrate the use of detailed kinetic computations to elucidate a chemical mechanism and to compute practical quantltles\nsuch as rocket performance, ignition delay times, and ignition lengths in flow\nprocesses.\n\nINTRODUCTION\nFor many years Lewis has been studying complex, reacting gas-phase systems at high temperature.\nThe changes that occur in such systems are the\nresult of many individual chemical reactions occurring at the same time.\nWe\nuse a two-step approach to understand what happens in such a system:\n(1)\nexperimental measurement of the temporal changes in temperature, pressure, or\ncomposition of the system, and (2) theoretical computation of these changes in\nan attempt to match the experimental results.\nThis report describes our work\nin the computation of complex-system\nchemical kinetics as focused on combustion systems.\nBrabbs describes our experlmental efforts in his paper for this\nsymposium.\nEarly attempts to compute the progress of complex reactions\ninvolved various simplifications.\nThese were of two types:\n(1) one or two\nglobal reactions were substituted for the actual set of many individual,\nsimultaneous\nreactions which occur in a gaseous system with several species\npresent, and (2) reactions involving some of the very reactive atoms and radicals were assumed to be very fast and, therefore, always in chemical equilibrium.\nDifferential equations for the rates of change of a few key species\nwere solved analytically.\nAlthough both of these approaches have had limited\nsuccess, they only explain some of the general features of a complex reaction.\nThey are, at best, simplified approximations\nof the actual process, which are\nonly valid under a limited set of conditions.\nThe most fruitful approach to\ncomplex kinetics computations\nis to numerically integrate a system of differential equations derived from the laws of conservation of mass, energy, and\n(if flow is involved) momentum.\nThese differential\nequations involve the\nrates of change of species concentrations\n_I (moles per unit mass of mixture) and temperature\nT, and they may also involve density\np and velocity\nV for a flow process.\nWhen these differential equations are solved, a set of\nindividual reactions is assumed to occur simultaneously\namong all the species,\nand these reactions are usually assumed to be reversible (with the ratio of\n\n175\n\nthe forward to reverse\nfor any reaction).\n\nrate constant\n\nbeing\n\nequal\n\nto the equilibrium\n\nconstant\n\nThere are two ways to use these detailed kinetic computations.\nFirst,\nthey may be used to elucidate a reaction mechanism or to determine a single\nunknown rate constant in conjunction with experimental data.\nOn the other\nhand, they may be used wlth a known mechanism to compute practical combustion\nquantities such as\n(1)\n(2)\n(3)\n(4)\n\nKinetic rocket or jet engine performance\nCompos_tlon of engine exhaust gases\nIgnition delays in combustible mixtures\nIgnition lengths and nozzle performance\nin supersonic\n\nExamples of some of these computations\nin this paper.\n\nperformed\n\nGENERAL\n\nat NASA\n\nLewis\n\ncombustion\nare given\n\nTHEORY\n\nA reacting system solve these equations.\nAlthough the differential equations are inherently\nstable, they have widely different time constants for relaxation to their\nfinal equilibrium solution.\nTherefore, the step sizes required for solution\nstability are prohibitively\nsmall.\nThese systems of equations are called\nstiff, and the problem of solving them accurately has been studied for many\nyears.\nSeveral new implicit integration methods have been developed for solving these stiff equations.\nAlthough they have been quite successful in\nimproving the accuracy and efficiency of the solutions, there is still a need\nfor additional development of better methods.\nProblems of accuracy and of\nefficient step-slze selection can still arise.\nMethods need to be developed\nfor automatic selection of the optimum integration parameters.\nTherefore,\nresearch is actively proceeding at many institutions to develop more efficient\nintegration methods (refs. l to 4).\nThere is another kinetic combustion model of interest to practical engine\ndesigners - namely, highly backmlxed reacting flow in what is usually called\nthe well-stlrred reactor.\nWe assume the limiting condition of useful first-order approximation\nfor some practical reactlng-flow systems.\nThe process is a constant pressure combustion with mass flow rate\nthrough a reactor of constant volume\nv. assigned\n\nOF CHEMICAL\n\nHydrogen-Oxygen\n\nBatch\n\nKINETIC\nReaction\n\nCOMPUTATIONS\nat Constant\n\nWITH GCKP84\nTemperature\n\nMeasurements\nof hydrogen peroxide formation In the constant-volume,\nisothermal reaction of hydrogen and oxygen at 500 \xc2\xb0C were reported many years ago\nby Baldwin (ref. 8).\nThe important reactions In the hydrogen-oxygen\nmechanism\nare as follows:\nH 2 + 02 = OH + OH\n\n179\n\nH\n2\n\n+ OH = H20 + H\n\nH + 02 = OH + 0\n0 + H2 = OH + H\n*H + 02 + M = HO 2 + M\nH + HO 2 = OH + OH\n*H202\n\n+ M = OH + OH + M\n\n*HO 2 + HO 2 = H202\n\n+ 02\n\n*H 2 + HO 2 = H202 + H\nThe rate constants for many of these reactions have been measured fairly\naccurately,\nbut some of the reactions involving H202 and HO 2 still have a significant uncertainty.\nThe reactions important in determining\nH202 concentration are indicated by an asterisk.\nGCKP84 was used to compute the H202 versus\ntime profile.\nAfter making several variations of the rate constants for the\ntwo most uncertain and important reactions (H 2 + HO 2 _ H202 \xc3\xb7 H and HO 2 +\nHO 2 _ H202 + 02), we obtained the agreement between the experimental and computed results shown in figure l. This example illustrates the use of kinetics\ncomputations\nin obtaining better rate-constant\nvalues for one or two key reactions when all other reactions in a mechanism are fairly well known.\nOf\ncourse, we cannot say that we have uniquely determined both reaction rate constants since we have two adjustable parameters.\nBut we have put limits on\ntheir uncertainty.\nOf course, this type of exercise can only be done when the\nexperimental\nvariable being matched by computation is sensitive to small variations in the uncertain rate constants,\nlhls brings up the following general\nquestion:\nFor a given change in any rate constant of the reaction mechanism,\nhow much does each computed variable change?\nThis is the relatively new\nfield of study in chemical kinetics called sensitivity analysis.\nIn practice, even through a chemical mechanism may contain more than lO0\nreactions, only the rate constants of 15 or 20 may significantly\naffect the\ncomputed results when their values are changed.\nRecently, methods have been\ndeveloped to systematically compute these effects in the form of sensitivity\ncoefficients\n(refs. 9 to ll). This computation is performed along with the\nchemlcal-klnetlcs\ncomputation.\nThe results of the latter computation are used\nas input to solve a second set of differential\nequations whose unknowns are\nsensitivity coefficients of the form\nkR ac I\n(12)\nSiR - cl ak R\n\nkRk m\n\n2\na ci\n\ncl\n\nakRak m\n\n(13)\nTiR m -\n\nThe flrst-order coefficient\nSiR\neffectively gives the percentage change in\nc i (Ith\nconcentration variable) for a given percentage change in the rate\nconstant of reaction\n_. The second-order coPfflclent\nTIRm\ngives the effect\n180\n\non\n\ncI of changes in both\nk_ and\nkm.\nIt is now recognized that a sensitivity analysis must be performed for any complex reaction in order to pinpoint\nthe important reaction paths and obtain a good understanding\nof the reaction\nmechanism.\n\nComputation\n\nof Rocket\n\nPerformance\n\nOne of the first uses of chemical kinetic computations was in the computation of rocket performance\nusing a one-dimensional\nflow model.\nThis performance is measured by the velocity of the hot gas at the nozzle exit point.\nAs the gas expands and cools, its velocity (i.e., kinetic energy) is kept as\nhigh as possible by chemical recombination reactions which occur in the nozzle. These reactions convert high-potential-energy\natoms and radicals into\nlow-energy stable molecules.\nMaximum performance is obtained if these reactions maintain equilibrium conditions in the very short nozzle-resldence\ntime.\nBut this is often not the case since the finite rates of the reactions are not\nfast enough.\nBy knowing the rate constants for the important recombination\nprocesses one can compute kinetic performance that more accurately and reallstically reflects the experimentally\nmeasured performance.\nAn example of this\nis shown in figure 2 for the oxygen difluoride - dlborane system (ref. 12).\nThe three curves of performance as a function of oxldant/fuel\nratio (fig. 2(b))\nshow the maximum and minimum theoretical performance\n(assuming equilibrium and\nfrozen conditions) as well as the kinetlcally limited performance.\nThe latter\nagrees rather well with actual measurements corrected for various losses.\nThe\nreaction mechanism used is shown in table I. This system is interesting\nbecause only one reaction rate constant has to be accurately known in order to\ncompute the kinetic curve.\nThis is the recombination\nof hydrogen atoms to\ngive molecular hydrogen.\nThis fact was determined by a simple sensitivity\nanalysis.\nComputed performance is unchanged for wide variations of the other\nrate constants.\nThe reason for this situation can be seen fromthe\nheat\nrelease data in table I. The hydrogen atom recombination\naccounts for well\nover 50 percent of the heat release in the process, and this, of course, controls performance.\n\nTwo-Stage\n\nWell-Stirred\n\nReactor\n\nThe practical use of the well-stlrred reactor model of GCKP84 is illustrated by its simulation of a two-stage turbulent combustor.\nA theoretical\nand experimental\nstudy of two-stage, rich-lean combustion as a method of controlling nitrogen oxide emissions was performed at Lewis.\nThis technique was\nsuggested as a way of reducing the conversion of the organic nitrogen compounds\nin hydrocarbons\n(fuel-bound nitrogen) to nitric oxide during combustion.\nThe\nobjective of the work was to study the effect of operating conditions on emlss_ons of both nitrogen oxides (NOx) and carbon monoxide (CO) (ref. 13).\nBoth the nitrogen and the hydrogen content of the fuel were changed to simulate coal-derlved syncrude fuels,\nlhese fuels contain high percentages of\naromatic hydrocarbons and therefore have a lower hydrogen-to-carbon\nratio than\ntoday\'s petroleum-based\nfuels.\nExperiments were performed in the two-stage\nflame-tube apparatus shown in figure 3. lhe fuels were mixtures of propane,\ntoluene, and pyrldlne blended to give a range of hydrogen and nitrogen contents.\nThe prlmary-zone equivalence\nratio was varied from 0.7 to about 1.8.\nlhe products of flrst-stage combustion were rapidly diluted with air injection\n\n181\n\nand burned at a final equivalence ratio of 0.5. The final NO and COconcenx\ntrations were measured. The two-stage flame tube was modeled analytically\nby\na two-stage stlrred-reactor\ncomputation closely simulating the experimental\nfuels.\nInstantaneous mixing of the secondary dilution air was assumed. A\ndetailed chemical mechanismwas used for propane and toluene oxidation and for\nNO formation. The fuel-bound nitrogen was input as nitrogen atoms and all\nx\nmixing and heat-transfer effects were neglected. This very simplified model\nof a quite complex process was able to predict most of the observed trends of\nNO and COformation with variation of nitrogen and hydrogen concentrations\nx\nin the fuel.\nThe model\'s prediction of other trends, which were not measured\nexperimentally, gave additional information about the advantages and disadvantages of using this rlch-lean combustion for emissions control.\nA comparison\nof someexperimental and computed results for NO and COconcentration as a\nx\nfunction of primary equivalence ratio is given in figures 4 and 5. The simple\nmodel qualitatively\npredicts the observed trends.\nIt can be seen that using a\nrich primary equivalence ratio does reduce NO formation very significantly.\nx\nHowever, at the sametime, this technique increases the formation of CO, which\nis also undesirable. Thus, one set of conditions cannot minimize both pollutants, and tradeoffs will have to be madeto obtain desired emissions levels\nfor any practical situation.\nThis work has shown that a simple kinetic model\ncan sometimesbe used to qualitatively\nexplain the observed trends in a highly\ncomplicated combustion system.\nIgnition\n\nDelays in Benzene-Oxygen-Argon\nMixtures\n\nOur current work involves the study of the mechanismof hydrocarbon oxidation.\nWeare studying the allphatlc hydrocarbons by measuring ignition delays\nand concentration profiles during the shock-heated oxidation of hydrocarbonoxygen-argon mixtures. Guo, Y.H. Zhang, and Q.M. Xie\nGas Turbine Research Institute\nCounty of Sichuan, People\'s Republic\n\nof China\n\nFour configurations\nof piloted flameholders were tested.\nThe range of\nflame stabilization,\nflame propagation, pressure oscillation during ignition,\nand pressure drop of the configurations were determined.\nSome tests showed a\nvery strong effect of inlet flow velocity profile and flameholder geometry on\nflame stabilization.\n\nINTRODUCTION\nPressure oscillation during the transient period from ignition to maximum\naugmentation\nis a critical problem for the turbofan augmentor.\nSince this\npressure oscillation may cause fan flow to stall or surge, it is important to\nknow the peak pressure during augmentor ignition and to take some kind of\ntechnical measure to reduce this peak pressure.\nSuch a procedure is called\n"soft ignition."\nThe combustion process in the fan flow of a turbofan augmentor is under a\nsevere condition.\nIt is helpful to understand how to make use of the hot combustion products in the core stream to ignite the combustible mixture in the\ncold fan stream.\nThe present research compares the capability for soft ignition and the ability of different configurations\nof piloted flameholders to\nignite a cold fan stream.\n\nSYMBOLS\nC\n\npressure\n\nF\n\narea\n\nGa\n\nalr-mass\n\nGf\n\nfuel flow\n\nh\n\ngap height\n\nM\n\nMach The airflow from the compressor\nIs divided Into two streams:\none stream goes to ejector 7 to create a lowpressure exhaust condition for the test section; the other stream goes through\nvalve l, flow-oriflce meter 2, air heater 3, control valve 4, and plenum chamber 5 into test section 6. After leaving the test section the combustion\nproducts are cooled by water injection.\nThe hot gas Is exhausted into the\nambient through the ejector.\nThe test rlg assembly Is shown In figure 2. It Is a rectangular duct\n300 mm by 175 mm. The piloted flameholder Is mounted inside the test section\nby bolts.\nFlame extinction Is recorded through a quartz window by a flamedetection device.\nFlame extinction can also be observed visually.\nThe ignition in the primary zone is provided by a hlgh-energy\nignitor of 12 J. The\ncombustible mixture in the secondary zone Is ignited by flame propagation\nfrom\nthe primary zone.\nThe inlet flow Is measured by the static pressure tap, the\nstagnant pressure tube, and the chromel-alumel\nthermocouple.\nThe outlet temperature Is monitored by a platlnum/platlnum-rhodlum\nthermocouple.\nOutlet\nstagnant pressure Is measured by three water-cooled\nstagnant pressure probes;\neach has three measuring points.\nThe transient pressure is measured by an\nInductlon-type\npressure transducer (CyG-l, 0 to l.O kg/cm 2) wlth an LR-I\ncarrier wave amplifier and an Sc-60 ultraviolet oscilloscope.\nThe fuel flow\nrate is measured by a turblne-type flowmeter wlth a digital frequency\nindicator.\nThe fuel Is injected through a spray bar. There Is one spray bar In the\nprimary zone.\nThe total length of the bar Is 280 mm.\nThere are slx pairs of\nholes (0.4 mm In dlam) on the bar at an intersection angle of 60 \xc2\xb0. In the\nsecondary zone there are slx spray bars.\nThe two bars In the center have four\npairs of holes (0.35 mm In dlam) at an intersection angle of 30\xc2\xb0; the other\nfour spray bars have four holes (0.35 mm In dlam) each.\nFuel injection In the\nsecondary zone is In the counterflow direction.\nThe test configurations\nof\npiloted flameholders are shown In figure 3.\n192\n\nTESTMETHOD\nThe test conditions were as follows:\nl_\n\n60 to\n\n70 \xc2\xb0C or\n\n500 \xc2\xb0C\n\nM2\n\n0.23\n\nP_\n\n0.25 to 1.2 kg/cm 2\n\nThe test procedures were as follows:\nFirst, the air supply was adjusted to\nmaintain the inlet conditions of P_, M2, and\nT_ as specified.\nAfter the\nprimary zone had been ignited the ejector air supply was adjusted to maintain\nP_, M2, and T_ constant.\nThen the fuel flow in the primary zone\nGfp\nwas\nincreased until fuel-rlch flameout occurred.\nAfter that, the _rlmary zone was\nreignited at the specified inlet condition and\nP_, M2, and\nT2 were kept\nconstant.\nFinally, the fuel flow was reduced until there was fuel-lean flame\nextinction.\nBefore ignition, the pressure drop between the inlet and outlet\nwas measured.\nDuring ignition the peak pressure, the fuel-alr mixture in the\nsecondary zone, and the pressure oscillation were also recorded.\n\nRESULTS\n\nAND DISCUSSION\n\nPeak Pressure\n\nMeasurements\n\nAs shown in figure 4, the peak pressure rise during ignition is usually\nlower than lO percent of the inlet pressure (AP2/P _ < TO percent).\nThe\npeak pressure rise in the augmentor is normally related to the amount of fuel\ninjected (as in these tests).\nSince the ignition is realized in a small volume, the amount of fuel necessary for ignition is small.\nThus, the peak pressure is lower than that for normal augmentor ignition.\nThis proves that using\na piloted flameholder makes it easier to obtain a soft ignition in a turbofan\naugmentor.\nPressure\n\nOscillation\n\nMeasurements\n\nPressure oscillation measurements were performed under the same conditions\nas the peak pressure measurements.\nThe amount of fuel injected in the primary\nzone has a very strong\neffect on the pressure oscillation\nin the augmentor\nduring ignition in the secondary zone (figs. 5(a) and (b)).\nIt has been shown\nthat, with the same amount of fuel injected in the secondary zone, the pressure\noscillation during the onset of secondary combustion varies with the amount of\nfuel injected in the primary zone.\nIt is clear that there is an optimum fuel\ninjection in the primary zone which minimizes the pressure oscillation during\nsecondary zone ignition.\nFlow Resistance\nFigure 6 shows the variation of the pressure loss coefficient with the\nReynolds number\nRe for different configurations.\nWhen\nRe\nincreases, the\npressure loss coefficient\nC decreases slightly.\nThe pressure loss coefficient\nC also decreases with the increase of gap height\nh. The pressure loss\ncoefficient\nC for a V-gutter is slightly lower than that for a semlspherical\nshape.\n193\n\nEffect of Inlet\n\nFlow Distortion\n\nlhe inlet flow distortion is shown in figure 7. Figure 8 shows the effect\nof inlet flow d_stortlon on flame stabilization.\nWhen the inlet flow is distotted, the range of flame stabilization\nis narrowed, and the peak value of the\nstabilization\nparameter is also reduced.\nFor instance, at\nP_ = 0.25 kg/cm 2\nwhen there is no inlet distortion, it is possible to ignite the secondary zone\nin 3 sec; but, when there is inlet distortion (even at\nP_ = 0.3 kg/cm2), the\nrange of flame stabilization is very narrow and the ignltablllty in the secondary zone is very weak.\n(It is impossible to ignite the fuel-alr mixture in\nthe secondary zone.)\nEffect of Secondary Air Entry Holes on Stabilization\nof the SJ410-4A Flameholder\nWhen 54 secondary holes (8 mm in diam) are opened, the range of flame\nstabi]izatlon\nis very narrow (fig. 9).\nWhen these holes are blocked, the\nstabilization\nrange is broader.\nThe secondary air entry holes also reduce the\ncapability of igniting the fuel-air mixture in the secondary zone.\nIt seems\nthat the secondary air dilutes the primary combustion products and reduces the\ntemperature.\nEffect\n\nof Gap Height\n\nFigure lO(a) and (b) show the effect of gap height\nh on flame stabilization for the SJ410-4B and SJ410-3A configurations.\nThe range of flame\nstabilization\ndecreases with the increase of the gap height\nh, and the Ignltability also decreases.\nFrom this test we found that, for a specified configuration, there is an optimum gap height.\nFor instance, when the width of\nthe V gutter is ?0 mm (SJ410-3A), the optimum gap height is 3 to 5 mm, the\nwidth of V-gutter is 40 mm (SJ410-4B), and the optimum gap height is 2 to 3 mm.\nUnder these conditions, the range of ignition and flame stabilization\nand the\nignition capability in the secondary zone are nearly the same for the two\nconfigurations.\nEffect of Bluff Body Shape\n\non Flame Stabilization\n\nFigure ll(a) shows the flame stabilization\nrange for the SJ410-3A configuratlon under ambient air temperature with a different bluff body shape.\nlhe range of flame stabilization\nof the V-gutter bluff body is wider than that\nof the semlspherIcal bluff body.\nBut as shown in figure ll(b), at T_ = 500 \xc2\xb0C\nthe ranges of flame stabilization\nfor both bluff bodies are nearly the same.\nIbis can be explained as follows.\nAt high inlet temperature, the vaporlzatlon\nrate of the fuel spray (before entering the primary zone) is h lgh for both\nflameholders;\ntherefore, the gaseous fuel-air ratios of the mixtures in the\nprimary zone are nearly the same.\nBut at low temperature, the fuel vaporl7_flnn\n\nr_f_\n\nnn\n\nfh_\n\nV_nllff:r\n\n\xc2\xa2llrf_ro\n\nic\n\nhinh_r\n\nfh_n\n\nfhmf\n\nnn\n\nfha\n\n:ami_(nharlr}l\n\nbody, and the fuel-alr ratio in the primary zone of the V-gutter\nthat of the seml-spherlcal body.\nlhus, the V-gutter has a wider\nstabilization.\nEffect\n\nof Air Entry Holes\n\nin V-Gutter\n\nWall\n\non Flame\n\nis higher than\nrange of flame\n\nStabilization\n\nFigure 12 shows the range of flame stabilization\nfor the SJ410-3B configuratlon with different air entry holes in the V-gutter wall.\nFor a given\n194\n\nopening area, fewer holes with larger diameters offer better flame stabilization than smaller holes. A comparison of figure lO(b) with figure 12 shows\nthat the reclrculatlon\nzone created by the gap provides better flame stabilization than that created by circular holes.\n-\n\nCONCLUSIONS\n\nThese tests led to the following conclusions.\n(1) The use of a piloted flameholder in the turbofan augmentor may\nminimize the peak pressure rise during ignition.\nAt the present experimental\nconditions, AP/P_\nis less than lO percent; therefore, the use of a piloted\nflameholder\nis a good method to realize soft ignition.\n(2) The geometry of the piloted flameholder and the amount of fuel\ninjected into the flameholder have a strong effect on the pressure oscillation\nduring ignition of the fuel-alr mixture in the secondary zone.\n(3) Compared with the V-gutter flameholder wlth holes in its wall, the\nV-gutter flameholder without holes not only has the advantages such as simple\nstructure and good rigidity but offers a wide combustion stability limit and a\nhlgh capability of igniting the fuel-alr mixture of the secondary zone.\n\nBIBLIOGRAPHY\nBranstetter,\nJ.R.; to bring the 9as sample to reactton\nconditions\nraptdly\nand homogeneously\nmakes shock-tube\nstudies\nof reactton\nklnettcs\nextremely\nattractive.\nIn addition\nto the control\nand uniformity\nof reactton\ncondltlons\nachleved\nwlth\nshock-wave\nmethods,\nshock compression\ncan produce gas\ntemperatures\nfar In excess of those tn conventional\nreactors.\nArgon can be\nheated to well\nover 10 stainless-steel\ntubing\n(5.7 m long,\n6.4 cm on a side,\n1.3 cm thick\nwalls).\nThe entire\nlength\nof the tube was ground to a constant\nInside\ndimension\nand then honed to a\nhighly\npolished\nflnlsh.\nStattons\nfor shock-wave\ndetectors\nwere located\nat\n15-cm Intervals\nIn the downstream portlon\nof the tube.\nTwo 2.5-cm calctum\nfluoride\nwindows were located\n25 cm from the end of the tube.\nA quartz\npressure transducer\nwas located\nat a posltlon\nwhere tt would provtde\nan accurate\nIndication\nof the ttme at whtch a shock wave arrived\nat the center\nof the windows.\nThe assembled tube could be evacuated\nto a pressure\nof about 1 um and\nhad a leak rate of less than 0.2 pm/mln.\nA 11quid nttrogen\ncold trap\nIn the\nvacuum line\nguarded agalnst\nthe back-mlgratlon\nof pump oll tnto\nthe shock tube.\nThe temperature\nand pressure\nbehind the Incident\nand reflected\nshock waves\nwere calculated\nfrom the measured\nveloclty\nof the Incident\nshock wave with\nthe\nshock program of Gordon and McBrtde\n(ref.\n3).\nSince the reactton\ntemperature\nhad to be calculated,\ntt was very Important\nto measure the lnlttal\ntemperature\nand the shock velocity\nas accurately\nas possible.\nThe distances\nbetween probe\nlocations\nwere accurately\nmeasured\nto four stgnlftcant\nftgures.\nThe shock-tube\nwall\ntemperature\nwas measured to \xc2\xb10.5 C, and It was assumed that\nthe test\nmlxture\nraptdly\nachieved\nthis\ntemperature.\nThin-film\nresistance\ngauges (1.3 cm\n207\n\ndiam) were used to detect the passage of the incident shock wave.\nThe amplified output from these gauges was displayed on a digital oscilloscope (see\nfig. l).\nData points were recorded every 200 nsec.\nSince with this scope the\nscale can be magnified to allow viewing of each point, we were able to measure\nthe incident shock velocity to better than \xc2\xb10.2 _sec (O.1 percent).\nAll data were taken at an initial test gas pressure of B.O0 kPa (60 torr)\nand a driver pressure of 792.7 kPa (ll5 psla).\nSoft aluminum diaphragms separated the hlgh-pressure driver gas from the test gas.\nThese diaphragms were\npressurized and then pierced by a gas-actlvated\npiston.\nThe shock strength\nwas varied by adding small amounts of argon to the helium driver gas to change\nits molecular weight.\nFigure 2 shows the variation of shock velocity with the\nreciprocal of the molecular weight of the driver gas.\nThe constant pressure\nratio across the diaphragm insured that the opening process for the diaphragm\nwas about the same for all tests.\nGas mixtures were prepared by the method of partial pressures in 34.4liter stalnless-steel\ntanks.\nThe gases used to prepare the mixtures had stated\npurities of 99.99-percent methane, 99.98-percent\noxygen, and 99.998-percent\nargon.\nThe partial pressure of methane and oxygen could be determined to\nbetter than 0.3 mm of Hg, and the total pressure of the tank to better than\n1.38 kPa (0.2 psla).\nAll samples were prepared to a total pressure\nof 413.6 kPa (60 psla).\nRadiation at 3700 A was monitored for each experiment and was displayed\non the oscilloscope with the pressure history.\nThe radiation was detected by\na photomultlpller\ntube whose field of view was restricted by two slits (0.75\nand 1.5 mm wide) located lO and 23 cm from the center of the shock tube.\nWith\nthis arrangement we could view a 3-mm slice of the hot gas in the center of\nthe shock tube at the same location as the pressure transducer.\nAn interference filter centered at 3696.5 A (with a half band width of ll5 A) was used to\nisolate the 3700 A radiation.\nThe filter transmitted\n51 percent of the incident radiation.\nExamples of kinetic experiments\nin the next two sections.\n\nSHOCK TUBE MEASUREMENTS\n\nconducted\n\nOF SPECIFIC\n\nin a shock\n\nREACTION\n\nRATES\n\ntube are presented\n\nIN THE BRANCHED-CHAIN\n\nH2-CO-O 2 SYSTEM\nWhen a mixture containing
