These mixtures\nwere chosen on the basis of (1) high sensitivity\nto the rate constant being\ndetermined, (2) minimized sensitivity\nto the other rate constants, (3) growth\nconstants in a convenient range for measurement(5xlO 3 to 7xlO4sec-l), and\n(4) sufficient\ncontent of minor, rate-limiting\nconstituents to permit accurate\nmixture preparation.\nIt was necessary to include in each mixture sufficient\nInfrared-actlve\ngas\nso that the arrival of the contact surface could be detected by the cessation\nof infrared emission. (The position of the contact surface between the driver\nand the driven gas was needed for the boundary layer analysis.)\nMixtures 2, 3,\nand 5 have substantial carbon monoxideconcentrations; carbon dioxide was added\nto mixture l to achieve adequate infrared activity.\nCarbon dioxide was also\nadded to mixtures 3 and 5 to ensure vibrational relaxation of the carbon monoxide. In preliminary experiments on a composition similar to mlxture 3 (both\nwithout C02) carbon monoxidewas not relaxed and rate constants gave an activation energy of 15 kcal for reaction 3; this is muchhigher than the values\nshown in reference ?. Results from mixture 3, with added CO show that the\n2,\npreliminary data were too low (particularly\nat the lower temperatures).\nCALCULAIION F RATE\nO\nCONSIANIS\nFROM\nEXPONENIIAL\nGROWTH\nCONSIAN_S\nRate constants were obtained from the experimental growth constants by\nmeansof equation (1), which was rearranged and solved for the rate constant\nbeing sought from the mixture in question: k2 from mixture 2, k3 from mixture\n3, etc. Mixture 2 was studied first, because the growth constants for this\nmixture depend almost exclusively on k2 and are only slightly affected by\nthe Fates of the other reactions (table I).\nValues of v4 were calculated\nfrom the k4 suggested by the Leeds Group (ref. 8). Trial values of kl, k3,\nand k5 were also taken from the Leeds recommendations(refs. ? to 9), but\nfinal values were taken from our owndeterminations.\nThis involves an Iterative procedure since our values of k3 depend, in turn, on our values of k2\nand, to a lesser extent, of k5. Two iterations sufficed to establish\nk2.\nNext, k3 was obtained by analyzing the growth constants for mixture 3,\nthen kI was obtained from mixture l, and finally\nk5 was obtained from\nmixture 5. Three iterations around the k3 kl-k 5 loop were required.\nA least-squares fit to the Arrhen_us equation was madefor each set of\nrate constants; these equations were then used in subsequent calculations.\nlhe experimental results are presented in the order in which the data\nwere obtained - first\nk2, then k3, kl, and k5. This is also the order of\ndecreasing precision.\nRate constants for reactions II and llI are plotted as functions of reciprocal temperature in figure 5. reactions which describe the ignition behavior of the hydrogen-carbon\nmonoxlde-oxygen\nsystem.\nWe have obtained what we believe are quite accurate measurements\nof the\nrates of the reactions H O.\n\nMETHANE\n\nOXIDAIION BEHIND REFLECTED SHOCK WAVES,\nMEASURED BY PRESSURE AND FLAME-BAND\n\nIGNIIION\nEMISSION\n\nDELAY\n\nTIMES\n\nLarge computers have enabled us to assemble kinetic mechanisms for modeling the combustion of many fuels.\nThese mechanisms may have as many as 150\nreactions (variables) and in some cases the only experimental\nparameter matched\nhas been the ignition delay times obtained from shock tubes.\nMany studies\nhave been conducted on the ignition of methane-oxygen\ngas mixtures in shock\ntubes.\nHowever, in some cases the data are in error because of probe location,\nand in other cases the data are badly scattered because of poor measurements\nof the incident shock velocity or a very noisy pressure trace.\nThe delay time measured for a shock-heated hydrocarbon-oxygen\nmixture is\nknown to be a function of initiation reactions, of reactions of radicals and\nmolecular oxygen with the fuel, and of branching reactions.\nThese branching\nreactions increase the radical concentrations\nto a level where ignition will\noccur.\nDuring ignition, there is rapid depletion of the primary fuel, very\nhigh radical concentrations,\nand an exponential\nrise in temperature and pressure.\nUsually delay times are measured somewhere in this region by techniques\nwhich follow some physical process such as change in pressure or in the appearance of some emitting species.\nAlthough the time difference between the start\nand completion of the pressure rlse Is small, one would llke to determine the\ntime of the appearance of the signal as accurately as possible.\nThis time,\nwhich is the start of the ignition process, allows one to separate the fast\nignition mechanism from the kinetics of the primary fuel.\nThe next section presents ignition delay data for the oxidation of\nmethane.\nData were measured with a quiet pressure transducer for the temperature range of 1500 to 1920 K. The quiet pressure trace allowed us to determine\nthe initial rise in the ignition pressure.\nThe delay times determined from\nthis initial pressure rise were compared with the time of appearance of the\n\n212\n\nradiation\nat 3700 A.\nIn addition,\nthe methane and carbon dioxide\nconcentrations\nat the time of ignition\nwill\nbe measured.\nThese parameters,\nwhich\nmust\nbe dupllcated\nby any kinetic\nmodel,\nw111 be used to test\nthe correctness\nof\npublished\nkinetic\nmodels\nfor the combustion\nof methane.\n\nRESULTS AND DISCUSSION\nIgnition\n\nDelay\n\nTimes and Probe\n\nLocation\n\nFrenklach (ref. lO) measured ignition delay times at two locations in a\nshock tube and concluded that the location of the probe must be taken Into\nconsideration\nwhen selecting delay-tlme data to be modeled by a kinetic mechanism.\nSince optical access was necessary to monitor the infrared and ultraviolet radiation behind the reflected shock wave, It was important to know the\nerror involved In measuring the delay tlme 7 mm from the reflecting surface.\nTherefore, two pressure transducers were located 7 and 83 mm from the reflecting surface.\nThese allowed us to simultaneously measure the delay tlme for\ntwo locations In the same experiment (see flg. 7).\nAn ethane-oxygen-argon\nmixture (I.27, 5.05, and 93.68 percent, respectively) was used for thls series\nof tests.\nFor these experiments, delay time was defined as the tlme at which\nthe ignition pressure exceeded\nP5 by lO percent.\nThls eliminated the\nguess-work usually involved In determining the point at which the pressure\ndeparted from the flat portion of the trace.\nFigure 8 Is a plot of the delay\ntimes for the two locations as a function of I/T.\nA curve drawn through the\ndata taken at the 83-mm position appears to show a constant difference In the\ntlme between the two locations.\nA clearer understanding of thls phenomenon can be obtained from figure 9,\nwhich Is a tlme-dlstance\n(t-x) plot of the trajectory of the incident and\nreflected shock waves near the end of the shock tube.\nAs can be seen, the\ntlme at which shock heating occurs at a given location along the tube Is a\nfunction of the reflected shock velocity and the distance from the end wall.\nThus, ignition would be expected to occur at the end wall first, since the gas\nthere has been heated much longer.\nWhen ignition occurs, the pressure rlse\ncauses a disturbance which propagates down the tube.\nThe mlnlnum propagation\nvelocity of thls disturbance is the speed of sound In the shocked gas.\nVermeer\n(ref. ll) took schlleren photographs of ignition behind the reflected shock\nwave, and they clearly showed that the disturbance\nmuch greater than the reflected shock wave.\n\npropagated\n\nat a velocity\n\nThe data In flgure 8 were used to calculate the propagation velocity\nbetween the two probes.\nThls velocity had an average value of 1660 m/sec,\nwhich Is a Mach number of about 2.5. Thls velocity Is about four times that\nof the reflected shock wave.\nUsing thls velocity one can estimate that the\ndelay times measured at the 7-mm position would be too short by about lO _sec.\nThls error would not be important unless the measured delay tlmes were near\nlO0 _sec.\nNo corrections have been made In the present data, but times less\nthan lO0 usec are not considered reliable.\nAnother disturbing feature of the pressure traces Is the amplitude of the\nignition pressure.\nUnless something changes, one expects the ignition pressure\nto be the same at all locations In the tube.\nHowever, the ignition pressure\nfor probe 2 Is much larger than that at probe 1 (fig. 4).\nThe ratio of the\n\n213\n\nignition pressure to the reflected shock pressure P5 for the two probe\npositions is plotted as a function of the delay time at probe l in figure lO.\nNote that this ratio is nearly constant at probe l but increases with decreasing delay times for probe 2. This behavior suggests that the gas properties\nat ignition are not the sameat the two locations.\nIgnition\n\nDelay Times\n\nIgnition delay times were measured behind reflected shock waves for the\nmixtures shownin table II.\nThe pressure history and the carbon monoxide\nflame-band emission at 3700 A were used to determine the onset of ignition.\nThe delay times measuredby both pressure and flame-band emission are recorded\nin table III.\nThese delay times \xe2\x80\xa2 may be correlated by an empirical\nrelation of the form\n(S)\n\n= A exp(B/R1)[CH4]a[o2]b[Ar] c\n\nLifshltz (ref. 12) and Tsubol (ref. 13) both found an overall pressure dependence of -0.7 for a , b \xe2\x80\xa2 c.\nPlots of TP 0-7\nas a functlon of I/_\nfor each\nmixture are shown in figure 8. Abstract\n\nA NASA - Chinese Aeronautical\nEstablishment (CAE) symposium on combustion fundamentals was held at the NASA Lewis Research Center from September 23 - 27, 1985.\nThis symposium was the result of an agreement (signed on 3anuary 31, 1979)\nbetween the United States of America and the People\'s Republic of China on\ncooperation in science and technology.\nA protocol agreement between NASA and CAE\nwas established\nin May 1983 to conduct exchanges and collaborative\nactivities on\nthe basis of equality, reciprocity, and mutual benefit.\nThe initial agreement\nwas to hold two symposia in the subject areas of structural analysis methods and\ntheoretical and experimental\nresearch in combustion fundamentals.\nThls publication Is a compilation of the technical papers presented at the second symposium.\nIt consists of five papers from senior specialists of CAE and seven papers from\nNASA senior specialists.\n\n17.
